“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
CONCERNING ENGLISH
CRITICS.
English literary criticism often seems to Aus-
tralians singularly bad. For one thing, it is
coloured by the loves and jealousies of literary
Cliques. Hath not one of their own writers re-
cently said that-“the bardlets of the Bodloy Head
sit in a row, and have pomatum rubbed into their
heads, week after week, by the assiduous Mr. Le
Gallienne, who will presently skip up beside them
again and expect to have pomatum rubbed into
him ” ? The Le Gallienne coterie makes a good
Jeal of current-literature criticism for the Daily
Chronicle and Weekly Sun —if that can be
called criticism which is mainly a chorus of un-
diseriminating praise. T. P. O’Connor’s criticism
in the Son is a bolus of long extracts washed down
with superficial epithets. The Literary World
Is weak and commonplace. The Bookman is
good, as far as it goes ; and the Academy is also
good, though tame. The ablest English literary
criticism, generally speaking, is found in the
Athen/EUM and the Saturday Review the
former being more thorough, the latter brighter.
Quiller-Couch, in the Speaker, is a good taster
and chronicler, but hardly a good critic. The
Spectator would be good if it could get rid of its
“ respectable ” bias ; the National Observer
runs too much to smartness and flippancy.
As an example of biased English criticism, take
the comments in the National Observer and the
Spectator on William Watson’s recently-pub-
lished “Hymn to the Sea." The former journal
sums-up the poem in two lines as “a long jingle-
jangle ” in “ metre both imitative and mono-
tonous.” The latter can hardly praise it enough in
two columns, and concludes by saying that “in
this noble poem, mistermed a hymn, but all the
more wonderful for the purely imaginative char-
acter of its splendor, we are greatly mistaken if
all competent critics will not recognise at last that
we have amongst us another of the really greatest
masters of English song.” Now, the truth, to an
Australian mind, seems to lie about midway
between these extremes of blame and praise.
And the Australian mind is so peculiarly-situated
that its judgments in such a matter are much more
likely to be sound than those penned in the heated
literary atmosphere of London. For its attitude
is impersonal, almost incurious ; it has the same
high standards to refer to ; and the dividing sea
lias almost the force of dividing time, and helps to
lift its dicta above the mists of contemporary
prejudice, into the clear upper-air which
strengthens and refines the verdict of posterity.
As examples of incompetent English criticism,
take almost every word penned regarding tales by
English authors with an Australia milieu —and
there is no English author so poor nowadays that ho
oannot sport a bushranger or a sundowner. Here
critics and authors generally browse in the same
fields of ignorance, with results satisfactory to
both classes. The Saturday Review, for in-
stance, says of one of Marriott Watson’s recently-
miblished stories, “The Last of
“ an Australian bush-ranger,” that it “would be a
striking story anywhere.” Anywhere—but in
Australia, where its exotic unreality kills it at
once. A bushranger sticks up a station. The
family are at evening dinner ; and “ beef or mut-
ton or pork ” are mentioned as their “ three
staples.” An English jackeroo has his meals with
the squatter, and is referred to by the writer (and
the bushranger) as “the cadet.” The squatter
resides on the station, and his manager. The
mutton is “half-bred Leicester.” The bush-
ranger enters and says all the hands are “ in a
corral.” “A station-hand, hot and dusty with
travel, broke into the room ” while dinner was
proceeding—as is apparently the habit of station-
hands. The bushranger takes the squatter’s arm
with one hand, and the manager’s with the other,
and marches them out to the stables. He is alotie
at the time ; both his hands are occupied ; and
they submit! The bushranger fires twice (joint-
blank without doing any harm ; and eventually he
and a mate are shot—thanks mostly to “the
cadet ” (of course). And so on. Perhaps one or
two of these things would pass singly; but
together, to an Australian eye, they make the tale
ridiculous.
But the Saturday Reviewer doesn’t know that.
RECENT PUBLICATIONS.
Ohas. H. Barton, author of “Outlines of Aus-
tralian Physiography ” (cloth, 3s 6d), is a well-
known Maryborough (Q.) identity; and what he
does is done thoroughly. His old-time leading-
articles in the Chronicle scorned to make any-
thing less than three or four 22-inch full news
■olumns. One in particular, discussing the ques-
tion of a bridge over the Burrum, or the Isis, or
some such trumpery little Northern creek, was the
awe of surrounding journalists. It started with
an historical exordium which referred to the
ancient stone bridges of China, Xerxes’ bridge of
boats, across the Hellespont, and Trajan’s big
bridge across the Danube, and gradually mean-
dered down the centuries past the Menai suspen-
sion, the Brooklyn, and the Tay, until it reached
r he Burrum bridge by very easy stages and urged
that tli" Govt., with all these noble examples
before them, should be urged to hand over £250
at once. The absolute accuracy of these details is
not guaranteed, but the impression of tremendous
learning was fix and on the reader’s mind in some
such fashion and 4i columns. Mr. Barton has
attacked Australian physiography with com-
parative brevity, but no less determination. His
book comprises th<> substance of a course of
University Extension lectures delivered last
year. He treats of the general configuration
-and hydrography of Australia ; of the climate ; of
hypsometrical and geological features ; of fauna
and flora ; and of the aborigines. His work has
chief value as an educational handbook ; its attrac-
tion for the general leader is slight. The style is
lucid, but not luminous ; the subject is treated
rather ponderously than picturesquely. The book
is admirable as an aid to study, but lacks interest
as an aid to popular information. This is not
meant as blame, but merely as admeasurement of
praise ; for Mr. Barton’s “ Outlines” can be cordi-
ally commended to Australians with intelligence
enough to appreciate them : the regret is only that
so many are unfitted to appreciate them —if a
deduction from the sales of piratical literature and
“ comic scraps ” has any value. An extract from
Mr. Barton’s most imaginative section —that
which deals with “The Aborigines”—would give
an idea of his best manner : elsewhere he is too
heavily-burdened with facts. But space is want-
ing, and the book should be bought. Only one
misprint is noted: “J. L. Jack” for “R. L.
Jack ” on p. 38.
The first number of Stead’s “ Penny Poets ”
series is to hand: “Macaulay’s Poems” (3d.).
It is a very cheap and creditable production,
though not so “ unique ” as Mr. Fitchett
thinks; for Dick’s penny series contains
many English classics, “ Merrie England ”
is a larger pennyworth than the “Lays,” and
Cassell and other publishers have done a good deal
with penny numbers. And these had not the
advantage of Stead’s mastery of reclame.
“ The Knife that Killed Po Haney ” is the title
of another set of Frank Stockton’s short stories
(cloth, 45.). They are cleverly done, but too
bloodless to interest much; and the humor of
“Rudder Grange ” is now attenuated to vanishing
point.
“The Avenger of Blood,” by J. Maclaren
Cobban (cloth, 45.), is a boys’ book of moderate
interest only.
More trash is published in Macmillan’s Colonial
Library than in any other of the series with which
Australia is favored. Books like “ Alice Lauder,”
and “The Burden of a Woman” belong to a
dull, commonplace type possibly interesting
to a very low-calibre intellect; but, in any
case, time is wasted in reading them when good
authors are so cheap and numerous, “ Thirteen
Doctors,” by Mrs. J. K. Spender—a series of
medical tales—is little better; and comparison
with “Round the Red Lamp” is inevitable and
disastrous. Frankfort Moore’s “Two in the
Bush ” is a collection of short stories which show
talent and are worth looking at, though the author
has done much better work. “ Two in the Bush,”
by-the-bye, is another injustice to Australia. It
is a bad English shot that can’t hit Australia.
“ Under God’s Sky,” is a provincial English char-
acter novel which calls for no enthusiasm.
George R. Parkin’s “Great Dominion ” is a series of
papers on Canadian commercial aspects, readable
and informing, and worth purchase by anyone
interested in the Canada of to-day. (The above,
each —paper, 2s. lOd. ; cloth, 3s. 10d.).
“Tales of Mean Streets,” by Arthur Morrison
(paper, 35.; cloth, 45.), has been very highly
praised in England. It is a series of stories and
sketches illustrating London East-End life, un-
doubtedly very strong, though the merit is rather
that of exceedingly clever journalism tiian of
literature, A book to buy.
NOTES.
Australians have much to be thankful for in
the matter of cheap books. In Bell’s, Macmil-
lan’s, Longmans’, Heinemann’s, Cassell’s, and
Methuen’s libraries we get the best new fiction at
about half the English price. Certainly, the
three-volume tradition has been ended summarily,
but English readers have often to pay Gs. for the
book which we have for 2s. >d. or 3s. fid. Dis-
count, indeed, lowers the over-sea price to 4s. Gd. :
but the growing fashion of net prices is seriously
weakening this pleasant custom of Paternoster
Row. Of course Australia pays the publishers for
her privileges ; for here nearly everyone has been
educated by the State into book-buying, and the
vogue of the circulating library is comparatively
small. To an Australian wholesale buyer said an
English publisher the other day: “ You have a
four-million population; but you buy, on the
English scale, books enough for ten millions.”
Mrs. Caffyn’s profits from “A Yellow Aster”
are set (on the best authority) at from .£120" to
<£ls'io. The publishers hardly expect to make
.£IOOO. The total sale of all editions to the end of
June was about 3 ,01) > copies— not so many, con-
sidering that the book was the rage of last London
season. The new and cheaper edition (45.) is
not selling well—the glory hath departed.
Australian booksellers have booked a large
number of orders for “ Trilby’’—wholly on the
strength of the English and American vogue.
Yet the book is rather dull than otherwise; and
the spicy Whistler passages which appeared in
Harper’s edition are cut out. For Du Maurier’s
“ Peter Ibbetson ” —quite as good, or bad—there
is no demand at all. And “ Trilby ” herself, mar-
vellous feet and all, is so long in reaching the Aus-
tralian public that when she does come nobody
will care, or even pretend to care, very much about
her.
Alleged Scotch “ wut ” is always sure of a
patriotic audience, and “lan Maclaren,” author
of “ Beside the Bonnie Brier Bush,” has no reason
to complain of the popular reception which has
welcomed his indubitable geese as swans of the
finest feather. A nation which can regard Burns’
Letters to Clarinda and Cos. as models of epistolary
English is capable of any literary aberration. But
“lan Maclaren,’’according to Douglas Sladen, who
reports his speech to an audience of authors, seems
to have more humor on his tongue than on his pen.
He said his boy came home from a public-school
and complained of being called “A Young
Pseudonym.” “If you are going to write books,
why can’t you write like this. Bee, this book is
something like. Here is a ghost, that’s all right;
there is a cliff and eagle’s eggs; there’s nothing
like that in yours. Here’s a big book swimming
with blood. See the number of people who have
to die. If you could write a thing like that I’d
read it myself, and I’d lend it to fellows in other
houses as well.”
Zola on Universities ; —“ Whoever has breathed
the air of such a school remains infected by it as;
long as he lives. The stale and sickly odour of
dead and useless learning clings to his very brain,
and in spite of all his efforts he goes through life
itching with petty jealousies and a pedagogue’s
love of the rod, and embittered with all the hatred
and envy of the old bachelor who had never had
courage enough to claim the woman he loved.
When such a fellow happens to be quick and bold
by nature, an innovator with new ideas, which is
not often the ease, he spends such time and labor
in giving his thoughts an academic and conven-
tional air, that we pass his discovery by as an
antiquated formula. He cannot be original if he
would ; he has lived top long in the mould ; his
being has taken the shape imposed upon it. You
may sow scholars and reap professors, but never
will you find an original, creative intelligence
among them pawns, pawns, nothing but
pawns.”
Angus and Robertson have arranged with
Henry Lawson to bring out a choice edition of his
works in two vols., prose and verse, at ss. each ;
posted, ss. Gd. Mrs. Louisa Lawson’s Is. edition
was exhausted some time .ago. The type and
binding will probably be uniform with those of
“The Banjo’s” book, to be issued by the same
publishers shortly; and the four volumes (Victor
Daley is also to be added) will form a substantial
contribution towards a desirable library of young
Australian authors.

We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business'we do,-and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not tar out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves „ _
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and oontentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surelv the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHON Y HORDE UN’S .
naver cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
(t has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

‘ A GOLDEN SHANTY.”
Bards of the Backblocks.
MY NATIVE LAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My native land, my native land 1
Your fruits are sweet, your gardens fair ;
Your fields expand on every hand
With harvest rich and vintage rare ;
Your hills are green, your skies serene,
A generous sun upon you shines,
And far-off lands with hungry hands
Are envious of your priceless mines.
Whoever seeks your genial shores
Your city “Queen of Beauty ” greets,
But hopeless hearts are at your doors,
And haggard faces haunt your streets.
My native land, my native land !
I hold it blazons your disgrace
That ruffian bands with greedy hands
Are mighty in each judgment-place.
The air is pure, the mart secure,
Earth yields its toll of wool and gold ;
But hide your face—’tis your disgrace
To know of justice bought and sold.
To know that hunger hovers o’er
The homes where hope must hold aloof,
That famine lingers near the door,
And sorrow shadows many a roof.
My native land, my native land !
The church-bells ring from gilded spires,
The brazen burst of noise ascends,
The incense from the altar blends
With vapor from the brew-house fires ,
The portly sweater in the kirk
Sits cushioned in the foremost row ;
His lean, exploited victims lurk
Where it would soil his garb to go.
His hymn is sung. His organ peals.
His plate goes round. His prayer is said, j
But does that soothe the man who feels
That someone robs him of his bread ?
Some day— perhaps toward the end
Of this r a m-cwm-religion age—
The sweater’s glory-hymn may blend
With sweater-victims’ hymns of rage,
When blood-stained, bent on vengeance comes
The haggard army from the slums.
My native land, my native land !
Why should these evil things befall ?
Each eager field would gladly yield
A lusty store of food for all.
It is not right that through the night
From shade to shade starved people glide
And fight with dogs to get the crusts
That richer hands have thrown aside.
That is, I hold, the crowning shame
Of shames that all your glories mar,
A blight to damn the fairest name
And brand your beauty like a bar.
My native land, my native land !
These be but feeble words we say ;
I would that in my vengeful hand
They might be swords to smite and slay
The wrongs that prosper here to-day.
My native land, my native land 1
Sydney.
CURLEW.
ROCK WALLABY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They sit at the doors of their rooky oastle,
Their bright eyes turned to the setting sun ;
Secure and calm in the peace of ages,
They hear the stream in the valleys run.
They take no heed of the world beyond it
Where human battles are lost and won.
From dizzy heights they can look around them,
And gaze on scenes we can never know —
The silver streak of the winding streamlet,
The topmost boughs of the trees below,
The crimsoned breast of a white bird flying
Towards the hills and the sunset’s glow.
They are full of secrets, these “ little people ; ”
They know of many a hidden cave; ,
They hear the call of the earth-bound river
To unknown shores that its waters lave,-
A sad, sweet song in the darkness fretting
For flow’rs and sun in its living grave.
Their young ones sleep to the gentle cadence,
The lullaby of the river-song ;
Their fathers heard it in long past ages—
It sings of joy when the pulse is strong,
And soothes the rest when the life-light flickers
Before the sleep that must last so long.
Perchance they know of the rich veins showing
Like lightning streaks in a stormy sky,
Of gems that sparkle in many a crevice
Not all the wealth of the world could buy ;
The secrets hid in the granite castles,
As “ silent people ” they live and die.
More fair to them are the dew-drops hanging
Like opals run on a silken string ;
Mure sweet the gold of the western heavens,
The rainbow tints on the bee-bird’s wing ;
More bright the sparkle of falling water
On silver sands at the mountain spring.
So they sit at the doors of their wind-swept castles
With calm eyes turned to the glowing west ;
The dingoes howl in the scrubs beneath them,
But they feel secure in their rocky nest.
And the tig-trees cling to the narrow ledges
Like posies pinned on the mountain’s breast.
You make me think, 0, you “little people 1 ”
Of old cave-dwellers beyond the sea,
Who lived and died and are quite forgotten—
For such your fate in the years must be.
But I pray for many a long day coming
The human rivers flow far from thee.
THANE.

SATURDAY, JULY 27, 1895.
The S.A. State Band Bank.
When the Seddon Ministry established the
State Loan Department in Maoriland, for
the purpose of advancing money on mort-
gage at minimum rates, and thereby relieving
agriculture from a heavy weight of usury,
that progressive province led the way, as it
generally does, in a much-needed reform.
South Australia, the only other province of
Australasia where a really democratic
Government is in power, is now following in
its footsteps. Victoria is also considering
some preliminary steps in the same direc-
tion, and there seems reason to hope that
the land-bank principle will soon be in
operation in three out of the seven states of
Australasia. In New South Wales, unfor-
tunately, the time ot the last three Govern-
ments has been almost wholly occupied in
refixing or demolishing, or trying to refix or
demolish, the tariff of their predecessors,
and the next Government seems likely to be
similarly occupied ; and in the Legislatures of
Queensland andWestralia the somnolent Fat
Man is predominant, and nothing new ever
happens, unless it be some new scheme for
the exploitation of the people in the Fat
Man interest. Meanwhile, the two provinces
where the land-bank scheme has already
taken definite shape supply a valuable object-
lesson to their neighbors if the latter are
capable of profiting thereby,
* * *
The State Loan Department of Maoriland
started with one enormous defect, from
which sprung a number of smaller drawbacks
and disadvantages. It was built entirely on
English capital instead of upon local
resources, therefore, despite the low rate at
which that capital was obtained, there is
another serious addition to the already heavy
outflow of money to meet the claims of the
great abseniee pawnbroker, J. Bull. The
loan falls due at a fixed date, although a
remote one, and, as future loans will probably
be arranged on the same basis, the State
land-bank is liable to be seriously embar-
rassed if the date of repayment comes at a
time of financial stress. The State*receives
its money in huge sums at long intervals,'
and their influx tends to create periods of
unhealthy inflation. It has to pay interest
on those sums until they can be safely in-
vested, and makes a certain loss in the
interval, and the operations of the Loan
Department are liable to be brought up
short by a sudden refusal on the part of the
nominee Chamber, which views the whole
proceeding with an unfriendly eye, to
sanction further loans. What is wanted
is not the sudden and violent arrival
of large slabs of borrowed capital, but a
steady and constant influx of fixed deposits
which can be regulated so as to make the
demand and the supply correspond, to some
extent at least, with each other. The
object of the institution should be twofold :
to lend money to agriculturists and others,
but principally to agriculturists, at the
lowest convenient rate of interest and with
a minimum of red-tape and legal expense,
and also to provide the safest possible form
of local investment. The Maoriland Loan
Department fultils the latter half of its mis-
sion only in so far that it furnishes a very
good investment for the savings of the Eng-
lish people, and leaves those of its own
country almost entirely out of the reckoning.
• # «
The scheme propounded by the Kingston
Ministry is better in some respects and worse
in others, but, on the whole, it looks the
more democratic and the more distinctly
commendable of the two. The Bill now
before the Assembly, however, is a huge,
unwieldy affair which should have been
divided into three. One part concerns the
reconstruction of the Government Savings
Bank. Another provides that the said bank
is to be the sole note-issuing authority in
the province, and supplies the necessary rules
and regulations. These two devices, how-
ever commendable, have no bearing on the
present subject. The third section of the
Bill provides for the creation of a State
land-bank with a maximum capital of
£3,000,000 —a woefully inadequate amount,
but one which will doubtless be increased
if necessary. This capital is to be raised
by the issue of 4 per cent, deben-
tures in sums of £lO each, which will
be transferable by delivery, so that they
will circulate as freely as bank notes. The
interest certainly looks excessive ; seeing
that the State lately floated a local loan at
per cent., the State land-bank should
be able to get its money at the same figure.
Also, it is announced that the bonds are re-
deemable in seven years, and if this means that
the date of repayment is definitely fixed
seven years hence, then there is serious
trouble ahead for Premier Kingston's new
institution. Also, it is definitely provided
that all profits are to be used to reduce the
rate of interest to borrowers ; the*S.A. Loan
Department, unlike that of Maoriland, is
not to be a money-making institution in any
sense ; and this Quixotic proposition wants
to be demolished with the axe of hard
common-sense, and interred in the place set
apart for defunct fads of all kinds. The
State land-bank should get its money at the
lowest rate possible in Australia, it being an
absolutely irrevocable condition of its exist-
ence that it takes local deposits only. It
should lend that money out at the lowest
rate which hard economy in its management,
and the necessity of accumulating a reserve,
will allow. But it should accumulate a
reserve, and keep on accumulating all through
its existence. Even an institution which
never lends more than half the value of the
security, and which insists on a steady and
continuous reduction of the principal of its
debt, is not safe from disaster in this
most uncertain of all possible worlds. More-
over, it should be the object of the institu-
tion to accumulate trading capital of its own
apart from that which is borrowed from the
nation, and to become a great State asset to
set against the State’s many liabilities.
* # . *
The necessity for a State land-bank is
sufficiently understandable when the present
condition of the agricultural and pastoral
industry is considered. Through a variety
of causes, the mere enumeration of which
would take far too much space at present,
country lands are just now, and have been
for a long time, and seem likely to be
for some years longer, the most absolutely
unrealisable form of property in Australia.
Money lent upon them, even under condi-
tions which make its ultimate loss next to
impossible, is almost certain to be locked up
for very long periods. Therefore, an ordi-
nary institution, whose funds consist mainly
of short-dated deposits, cannot safely lend on
such security. The Australian banks which
are now either avowedly or unavowedly in
more or less serious difficulties—and this
includes nearly all the banks in this country
—owe a good portion of their troubles to the
fact that they borrowed money from the
public for terms ranging from three months
to five or six years, and invested much of it
in mortgages where it may not be recover-
able for a quarter of a century. The high
rates which they charged, partly in
view of this risk and mainly through
blind avarice and incapacity to take a
logical view of anything, helped to accelerate
the recent fall in values, and to make their
money still less recoverable ; and their frantic
efforts to extract payment from their debtors
after the crash of 1393 caused further depre-
ciation, and made the situation all the more
hopeless. The big life insurance societies
are able—as an insurance society cannot
well be rushed by creditors—to wait much
longer for a recovery, but even they cannot
afford to lock up money indefinitely ; and
the private mortgagee is generally in much
the same situation. Therefore, it has come
to pass in New South Wales, and in a less
degree in other provinces, that the man who
wants to borrow, up to half or even less than
half the present value of a purely agri-
cultural freehold, in this period of excessive
cheapness, has often to pay as much as 10
per cent, for the accommodation, and some-
times finds it difficult to procure even at
that exorbitant figure. Agricultural pro-
perty is so slow of realisation that the lender
must be prepared either to let his loan
stand for an indefinite period, or to foreclose
and take a very indefinite and far-off chance
of realisation. Therefore, a state of things
has arisen under which the most toilsome
and worst-paid industry in the land is
weighed down with the heaviest load of
interest, and one which it is utterly unable
to carry. The remedy obviously lies in
the establishment of an institution which
has an enormous amount of capital to
lend, which can lend cheaply because it
obtains its capital at a low rate, and which
can lend for very extensive periods so long
as it is covered by abundant secuiity, because
its capital is repayable solely at its own con-
venience. And the reason why the land-
bank should be a State instead of a private
institution is that the State can borrow—
and consequently lend—more cheaply than
any private company. Also, a further
reason is that, even under the most careful
system, a certain number of properties must
fall into the hands of the bank by foreclosure,
and the accumulation of property in the
hands of a joint-stock institution would be
a further aggravation of the evil of private
landlordism.
* * •
The Bulletin, therefore, suggests the fol-
lowing conditions as a basis for the State
land-banks, when these institutions definitely
arrive in Australia : (1) That their capital
should have no fixed limits and should be
raised by the issue, within Australia only, of
£5O, £2O, and £lO debentu res, bearing
interest of per cent., or less, if money be
procurable at a lower figure, and redeemable
at the option of the State only, at any time
after 10 years. (2) That the money thus
obtained be lent on freehold land, agricul-
tural land always having the preference, to
the extent of not more than half its value, at 5
per cent, interest, with either 1 or 2 per cent,
added for the repayment of the principal.
The borrower would thus wipe off principal
and interest by a payment of six or seven
percent., whereas he now pays more than
that rate for interest alone, and the constant
reduction of the total amount outstanding
would protect the institution, in a great
measure, from any possible loss through
depreciation of the security. (3) That all
work in connection with valuations and the
preparation and release of mortgages be
done by the bank’s own officers at the lowest
possible rates, which should be about one-
third of those now charged by the ordinary
legal shark. And (4) that out of the differ-
ence of £1 10s. per cent, between the bor-
rowing and lending rates, 10s. per cent, be
set aside as a reserve, one-half of it being
employed in new loans and the other half
invested in Government securities as a pro-
vision for unforeseen emergencies, and that
all profits arising from the investment of the
reserve be further reserved in the same
fashion. The other £1 per cent could be
made to cover all working expenses even at
the outset, and if these can be reduced-
and they should be reducible in course of
time by one-half—then of the amount thus
saved one-half should allotted to borrowers
in reduction of their debts, and the other
half go to the reserve. When the State takes
over the issue of notes, that issue might be
made through the land-bank, and the profits
arising from it be also added to the reserve.
* • m
The debentures of the State land-bank
would probably have almost the same cur-
rency as bank-notes, and in times of financial
panic they could be made to answer the
same purpose as a temporai-y legal tender
note-issue. A considerable amount of the
gold-reserves now lying unprofitably in the
coffers of the banks would doubtless be in-
vested in these securities, and between the
banks and private lenders the amount of
money locally available should be fully equal
to, if not in excess of, requirements. The
operations of the institution would relieve
the agricultural class of a heavy load in in-
terest, and thereby make agriculture more
profitable ; they would help to prevent future
banking panics by taking this class of busi-
ness to a great extent out of the hands of
private institutions, whose interference in it
has been woefully disastrous to ail parties
concerned ; and by helping the agricultural
industry they would increase the value of
country lands, and thereby enrich the small
independent landholder. Also, if not coun-
teracted in this respect by other measures,
they would increase the value of the land
shark's big estate, and thus enable him to
charge heavier rents to his miserable tenants.
Consequently, the old moral once more arises
that the large landowner, and still more the
large landholder who does not cultivate his
own property but lets it out to tenants, and,
above all, the large landholder who does
this, and spends his rents abroad as an
absentee in addition to his other sins, re-
quire to be struck by a tax which will effec-
tually discouiage their particular branch of
capitalism. Otherwise the land-bank system
would indirectly tend to increase the burden
of rent and to help on the accumulation of
big estates, by making the occupation of the
big land-grabber mure profitable than id
now is.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Tobacco Monopoly.
Mr. J. M. Sinclair, one of the many commis-
sioners that Victoria sent abroad in the days of
'Patterson and his predecessors, lias come back
with a valuable idea—about the only one which
the country has received in return for much
expenditure. He proposes that Victoria should
follow the lead of France, Italy, and other
European countries by making the manufacture
and sale of tobacco, cigars, cigarettes and snuff
—if there is any demand for snuff nowadays—a
State monopoly. The idea is regarded as dis-
tinctly “un-English,” and, therefore, it has come
with a considerable shock on the community,
but it has much to recommend it, none the less.
In France the tobacco monopoly yields a clear
profit to the Government of .£12,000,000
annually. In Austria-Hungary it returns a
profit of about £00,000,000, and in many other
countries it works satisfactorily and furnishes
a substantial revenue. Considering the heavy
consumption in Victoria, the monopoly should
supply a revenue to the Melbourne Treasury of
at least .£600,000, at present wholesale and
retail prices, whereas the present tobacco-tax
returns only half that amount, and the differ-
ence is an item of very considerable import-
ance. It is also one which will increase with
the growth of population. The great ques-
tion now in all the Australias is to
obtain more revenue—much more revenue,
so as to construct public works without
borrowing, and to remedy the eternal
deficit, and yet to pile as few new taxes as pos-
sible on an aggravated nation which generally
rejects a heavy - taxing Government at the
earliest opportunity. Tobacco and drink mono-
polies, a iState Land Bank, and a State paper
currency are all possible sources of income
which would imply no visible taxation, and as
such they are just the sort of thing wliich the
hard-driven public financier craves after. And,
in addition to this, the tobacco monopoly has
certain collateral advantages. The State, as
the sole manufacturer and wholesale dealer,
would buy its supplies of raw material as far as
possible from the local producer, at a fixed
price, on well-defined conditions. A certain
standard of quality would lie insisted on, and
if the local grower exerted himself to reach
that standard, the State would take all his
product up to the limit of its requirements, and
give him the preference over the importer.
Thus, provided he took sufficient pains to turn
out a high-class article, lie would have a con-
stant and reliable market provided for him; if
he didn’t take the necessary pains the business
would go to the cheapest foreign producer who
did. The State tobacco monopoly would
thus give immense assistance to the local
grower if he was worth assisting, and its
factories would pay to local workmen a
large amount in wages which now goes abroad.
Also the system would ensure that the
goods sold would be of good quality, if the
nation was not too tired to insist that they
should be so. And, for the benefit of that large
section of the community which is British above
all things, it is worth noting that the tobacco
monopoly—like a good many other things—is
not quite so “ un-English” as is generally sup-
posed. In India, where the British officials had
a comparatively free hand to work according to
the dictates of common sense, the largest source
of revenue is the land-tax, the next largest the
State railways, and two other important items
of revenue are the profits of the State opium
monopoly and of the State paper currency.
The difference between an opium and a tobacco
monopoly is merely a difference of circum-
stances.
MacLaurin on Land-Tax.
One of the objections to the N.S.VV. land-tax
so eloquently advanced by Dr, MacLaurik,
M.L.C., was as follow’s ;
The real object of the Government, so far as I can find
out, if they understand their object at all, is to impose a
tax which would be more severe upon the country than it
is upon the town, and for the following reasons; if any
one will look through ilr. Coghlau’s last bonk he will find,
if he makes a comparison between the people in municipal-
ities which are practically town populations and the people
outside municipalities, that in the municipalities the value
of the land is al)out£78,000,000, and the value of buildings
and improvements about £72,000,000. That is to say,
practically speaking, the lands and improvements are of
equal value. On the other hand, in the country outside the
municipalities, the value of the land is about £97,000,000,
and the value of the improvements £57,000,000, of which
£9,000,000 worth are on Crown lands, leaving £48,000,000
worth on private lands. That is to say, outside the
municipalities the value of the lands is to the value of the
improvements as twm to one. What is the result? The
result is that a person who lives in the town pays Id. in
the £ on half of his property, whereas the man that lives
in the country pays Id. in the £ on two-thirds of his pro-
perly. That is the real boiling-down of the Bill.
This is a Hue example of the kind of whizzing
noise that does duty for logic. The proposal to
tax land at tne same figure all round is inequit-
able, according to Dr. MacLaurin, because tbo
town inhabitant has pub more improvements on
his property, ‘and paid for them, than the
farmer. And, on the same basis, the land-tax
would be inequitable if there were more cross-
eyed peopfe, or men with bow-legs, to each acre
in the city than in the country, “Also, the dog-
tax is inequitable because there are more dogs
to the square mile in Sydney than there
are out by the Bar won. The MacLaurin
theory, boiled down into very inferior tallow,
and carried to its logical conclusion, practically
amounts to this : that the same thing shouldn’t
be taxed at the same rate throughout the coun-
try, unless the thing that stands on top of it is
of the same value in each case. Therefore, the
land in George-stfeet, Sydney, which has the
Equitable building on top of it, shouldn’t be
taxed on the same scale as a piece of equal
value in the same street with a two-storey
shanty on it. Likewise, the man with a straw
hat on top of him as an improvement should
pay less taxes than a man with a bell-topper.
Every argument wants to be stood on its bead
and inspected both ways up before it is accepted.


Gambling' is Scriptural.
Dear Bulletin,-— Victorian parsons cry aloud
against gambling, and their cry is chorused
stertorously by the “books.” But what is the
verdict of the Book ? In Leviticus (xvi.), Aaron
“tosses up” (or oasts lots) witli God for the
scapegoat. In Numbers (xxvi.), the land by a
Mosaic law is divided on the art-union principle.
In Joshua (vii.), capital punishment is inflicted
on the verdict of dice, or some similar exemplar
of innumerable possibilities, and the victim
confesses the crime. In Samuel (x.), a king is
chosen “by lot”—and he was as big a failure
as other kings. In Acts (i.), a capable apostle
is chosen in the same way. The words of
Solomon on the subject are—
The lot is cast into the lap: but the whole disposing
thereof is of the Lord. . .
Gambling was a pastime of the Jews, but
nowhere is it condemned in their Scriptures.
Usury, “ sweating,” false weights, hard drink-
ing, monopoly, land alienation, etc., etc., got
heavy knocks from Moses and the prophets—
but gambling goes unchid. Whence get the
parsons their brief ? The bookmaker is perhaps
the devil’s advocate, and he barracks with the
parson now. Is gambling in itself vicious?
Giffen and Stoddart “ toss .up for first
innings in an “International ” —is this wicked?
Four bishops play whist —3d. a corner ; what
moral law is violated? John Smith put a
pound he can spare on the tote —is he to be
kept out of heaven by the parsons, and boy-
cotted in hades by the bookies ? The parsons
and the pencillers they toil not, but they do
spin yarns. There can be excess in gambling
just as there is excess in parsons. Excess is the
trouble. The bookmaker’s cause is a leak in
the economic boiler, but the tote is a safety-
valve for exuberance ! The parsons’ idea seems
to be to stop up leaks, and then run the boiler
without a safety-valve.—F.
Where the Assets are.
Any one who cares to form an idea how the
selector and the individual pastoralist are being
wiped out in New South Wales by those
eternal mortgagees, the banks, and how the
country is getting along to that fine British
condition in which everyone will work for a
landlord and no one will work for himself,
should study the Government Gazette. The
pages of that cold and business-like publication
are full of information to the reader with a
seeing eye and a large, anxious ear. Here are
a few examples taken at random from the
latest issues : On pages 4T27-34 appears a list
of certain pastoral leases in the Central Divi-
sion on which extensions have been granted.
There are 486 squatting properties on the list,
and 244 of these are registered as belong-
ing to banks and finance companies. On
pages 4185-88 appear sundry lists of con-
ditional purchases for which it is intended
to issue certificates. Out of 31 properties
on one list (page 4186) nine belong to
banks and one to a Scotch insurance society.
There are 38 names on the next list, and they
include seven banks and one finance co. Out of
49 selections on page 4187, 12 belong to banks,
but out of 35 selections on page 4188, the banks
hold only six. There are nine conditional pur-
chases in a list on page 4173, and of these one
bank owns four. On page 4025, it is announced
that certificates are to be issued upon 40 condi-
tional purchases, of which eight are held by
banks. Thirty-eight selections are mentioned
in a similar list on page 4027, and of these 11
are in the hands of banks and finance com-
panies. On page 4085 appears a list of 31 selec-
tions, portions of which are to be resumed for
roads, and of these various banks appear as the
registered owners of seven. The Gazette of
4th July announces that 208 “preferential
occupation licenses ” have been granted
in the Central Division, and 56 of these
are granted to banks and financial com-
panies. Nine out of 50 selections on page
4333, for which certificates are to issue,
are in the hands of the same eternal mortgagee,
and seven out of 29 on page 4331. The trail of
the bank is over the entire pastoral and agricul-
tural industry of the province. Probably few
shareholders have any idea how much of their
alleged assets consist of hundreds of little
isolated patches of land dotted over all parts,
even the most remote, of this great loan land.
If they knew the story in its entirety they
would probably hang their scrip on a willow-
tree, and weep bitterly, and refuse to be com-
forted.
A bill now before Maoriland Parliament pro-
vides that any person owning a house and land
to a value not exceeding ,£l5OO can settle them,
by a very inexpensive process, on his wife and
children, and after the settlement has been six
months in force the property is absolutely pro-
tected against all creditors. The old man has
the use of it during his lifetime, but at his
death the settlement takes effect. This is good
enough so far as it goes, but, like a similar
scheme propounded in New South Wales, it
doesn’t go nearly far enough. It is intended
principally as a protection for the agricultural
settler, and that person is just the one whose
chronic ignorance of law and natural inertia—
not to say lumpishness—will prevent him
making adequate use of it, while the city specu-
lator and the professional landlord and all the
bird-of-prey element will adopt it liberally.
What is required is a law providing that every
man’s freehold home and—if he is an agricul-
turist or pastoralist—a certain number of acres
attached thereto, shall be absolutely protected
against seizure by any creditor, the Govern-
ment alone excepted, for any debts, on mort-
gage or otherwise, incurred after the passing of
the Act, without any registration process what-
ever. Every man, when his existing mortgage
is wiped off, would thus be prevented from in-
curring a new one so far as his actual place of
residence is concerned, and nobody could
acquire the right to eject anybody else and his
family into a stony world. The hitherto in-
alienable right of one man to “ sling out ”
another has been recognised altogether too long.
The most persistent and prevalent feature of
politics, and in politicians, is inconsistency.
Almost every prominent Freetrader in N.S.W.
Assembly has been, at some time or other, a
clamorous Protectionist; almost every Protec-
tionist has been a Freetrader. Many of the
Opposition, both in Assembly and Council, who
the other day voted against land-taxation,
loudly advocated it a year or two ago. Half
the measures now moved by the Reid Govt,
and opposed by the Dibbs party were, two
years ago, moved by the Dibbs Govt, and
opposed by the Reid party. “ Hi don’t want
hoffis ” one week ; Hi lead a forlorn hope to
grab hoffis the next. The Joe Chamberlain
of old becomes the honorable aristocrat and
Tory colleague of now. Parkes of 42 years
ago hurled “ Brummagem House of Lords ” at
Wentworth, then an ex-patriot turned Tory
and groveller ; now, Premier Reid flings the
phrase at Parkes, himself turned boot-polisher
and champion of a Tory Upper House. Corol-
lary : Let any man keep a full and truthful
diary for ten solid years, and, at the end of the
decade, look back at what he idas and see if he
can identify that with what he is.
Some impecunious Treasurer might seriously
consider tile advisability of raising the minimum
fine for the common or garden drunk to 205.,
and for t he drunk with trimmings to 405., with
corresponding increases for bad language,
common assault, and breakage. Victoria could
raise £30,000 a year easily from this source,
and N.S.W. £50,000. Another valuable idea
would be to add to each line (say) 25 per cent,
additional costs as payment for the use of the
court and magistrate. This particular aspect
of the costs question hasn’t been considered so
far, yet when a ss. drunk gets the use of a
£5OOO courthouse to be fined in, it is only reason-
able that he should pay for it. The drunk and
the language and assault persons are neglected
sources of revenue. Still another idea would
be to let off the more affluent minor offender
without any money penalty at all, but to
sentence him to take up interminable 2£ per
cent. State debentures for, say, 20 times the
amount of his lawful tine. As a means of
raising money cheaply in the country there are
some valuable points about this idea, and Aus-
tralia might ultimately pay off its foreign
indebtedness out of its drunks and disorderlies
and other small criminals. The drunk, in
short, is a mine which wants to be properly
worked, and it is incomprehensible that, while
politicians have been at their wits’ end for
money any time this last 10 years, he should
have been neglected.
The case of two young men arrested at Colling-
wood (Vic.) recently is of Australasian interest.
They were unimportant young men, and were
arrested confessedly for doing nothing. No
man with a shabby coat and patched pants can
safely do nothing in Melbourne. The arresting
constable deposed that there had been a number
of robberies in Colling wood lately, and he ran
in the two young men in question in consequence
—not because there was the remotest visible
connection between them and the robberies,
but because they seemed to come handy, and it
was as well to run them in as anybody else.
Anyhow, the policeman had nothing else to do
at the time, so it didn’t matter. A liigh-piriced
lawyer was called into requisition, and he
proved that the prisoners had been working up
country recently, but had been unable to get
anything to do since their return to town, be-
cause they couldn’t well look for work whilst
they were locked up for not having work ; but
they were very sorry, and wouldn’t do it again.
In consideration of these expressions of contri-
tion, the Bench took a lenient view of the case,
and discharged the prisoners severely. The
young men then paid their lawyer and went
home. The next time a man is arrested for
being out of work, The Bulletin hopes all the
employers in the vicinity will be charged with
being accessories before the fact. It would be
strictly logical.
The N.S.W. Government has made a tolerably
bad beginning with its perpetual leasing system,
provided for under the new Lauds Act. The
first soil thrown open under its provisions is out
in the West, in the land of Goo-forgotten,
about nine or ten miles from Bourke. It is
irrigated from an adjacent bore, and the rental
is estimated on the very high capital value of
£2O an acre. Another experiment is being
made at Temora with almost waterless land,
which the department values at from
255. to 40s. an acre. The perpetual leas-
ing system isn’t likely to amount to much
under these conditions. If it is to have
anything like a fair chance the Govern-
ment should include in its Land Tax Bill,
when it is next introduced, a clause by
which it can resume large estates at a small
percentage over the tax valuation, and then try
its perpetual leasing system in the Eastern
Division, where markets are more adjacent and
the drought spectre doesn’t stalk about so
persistently. As The Bulletin has said over
and over again, it is not the Central Division
which so badly wants to be opened up as the
Eastern one. There is enough land there lying
unused in the hands of Fat Men and speculative
syndicates to meet the demands of agriculturists
for some years to come, and the policy which
allows the Fat Man and the syndicate indi-
vidual to block settlement in the coastal regions,
and shoves the agriculturist far out into the
dismal West, doesn’t give the latter anything
like a fair chance. A perpetual lease at an
annual rent of 10s. per acre, in the vicinity of
Bourke, is about as dismal a thing as any man
could have.
A Melb. Herald genius describes Kev.
Haskett Smith’s lectures :
He tells the story of his travels, and does not really
lecture, and draws a vivid mental picture, which is splen-
didly supplemented by the very excellent views which
are presented. Mr. Haskett Smith took his audience to
the hill of Calvary, where the crucifixion is held to have
taken place ( sic!), the Pole (sic!) of Si loam, and the
Mount of Olives, keeping their interest unshaken (sic !)
while he discoursed upon the sacred stories attaching to
these historic spots.
The crucifixion being “held to have taken place”
at Calvary is delightful from a paper the man-
aging editor and half the directors of which are
Catholics. And the “Polo of Siloam !”
Stephen Stambouloff, the Bulgarian ex-
Premier, who was fatally hacked by a gang of
assassins, the other day, in the streets of Sophia,
supplies a fine example of national ingratitude.
When Bulgaria came into political existence
about 17 years ago it was little better than a heap
of ashes with a starving community rooting tor
food among the ruins. Stambouloff—the one
brainy, iron-handed patriot among a crowd of
nonentities—re-built its ruined towns, organised
its finances, created an army strong enough to
wipe out the invading Servians, established
a liberal system of education, and contrived,
with the very narrow resources of a very
miserable little kingdom, to stave off Russia
on one side and Turkey on the other. He
propped up Alexander all through his stormy
reign, and when that gaudy Battenberg gave up
the struggle, and Russian annexation was im-
minent, he made himself practically dictator, and
broke up the pro-Russian movement. Then
Panitza organised another Russian conspiracy
with a great part of the army at his back, and
Stambouloff, when everybody else was paralysed
and the country was degenerating into a reign of
terror, cut the matter short by arresting him with
his own hands, and hauling him away to prison
and execution. But at last the Bulgarians grew
tired of their one great statesman, and Ferdinand,
the scented prince and botanist, with the 150 em-
broidered shirts, sacked him for not possessing
sufficiently elegant manners and for a general
want of respect to his royal master. He be-
came virtually a prisoner, not being allowed
to leave Bulgaria. He was tried on various futile
charges. The mob in the street, incited by
Russian agents, threw mud and stones at their
national hero. He was ineffectually shot at on
sundry occasions by conspirators who could not
aim reasonably straight, and he became pre-
maturely old and a hopeless wreck from diabetes.
Finally, he was fatally mangled, in broad day-
light, by four assassins, and died with both his
hands cut off and 2G wounds on his head and else-
where. Stambouloff was probably the most
picturesque patriot Europe has produced since
Garibaldi, and his tenth-rate country throws a
dejected little brick at his memory by way of
reward for all he did on its behalf. It also threw
bricks and mud at his funeral, and his coffin had
to be protected by the police.
“In there a God?"
Tlie poet, like the woman, must be new ;
He must be up-to-date ; the world to-day
H as reconstructed—like the banks —its view
Of what the virtues are to which we pay
Our deepest rev’renee ; where of old the true
Was valued, now the plausible holds sway.
Two gods sit high enshrined ; the servile swear
By these alone, “ My Lord,” “ Sir Millionaire.”
For love of good we write the love of gold,
And chivalry is spelt K.C.M.G. ;
Where freemen might the law for freemen
mould
The shrill promoter floats his company,
The sham reformer’s for a title sold,
And in one mighty chorus all agree :
“ So he be not a lord or have no purse,
Ho w can he be deserving of a verse ? ”
“ My Lord” —Good Lord!—a hundred years ago
A great man was, and then became .1 peer ;
His greatness he belittled ; did he know
That with himself the brains would disappear
From out his stock and leave an empty show,
A shell, a name, the subject of a jeer
When Lord Tomnoddy’s hobby we report ?
Skirt-dancing ’neath the lime-lights is his forte.
Ye slaves of custom ! will ye never learn
The man’s own actions measure real worth ?
Why can ye not the staring truth discern
That he who boasts of nothing but his birth
And trades on it to play the fool, or turn
To vice unchecked, or vacant drunken mirth,
Or deems him fashioned out of finer clay,
Is but a rotting branch best cut away ?
What faults, what follies oft the name con-
dones !
A royal courtesan, that flaunts her shame :
A title may be won by timely loans;
Beer, if one brew enough, will do the same ;
A cmcp which, lost, will end in breaking stones
Gets Mr. Jones a handle to his name.
On grounds like these descends from sire to son
The rank upheld as nobly as ’twas won.
And here to southern skies the taint has spread;
We have our “honors,” too (God save the
mark !)
The “ golden link,” the Governor, at the head,
Whose levees form a sort of social ark
Where knights of dubious grammar proudly
tread
Beside their would-be comrades, the fat shark
Of many acres, and the politician
Whose wife declares a title is his mission.
By this great deity there sits one more
Who’s greater still and still more widely
wooed ;
Rank all men worship ; gold they all adore,
All-potent'wealth, whose worshippers include
Saint, sinner, worldling, parson, wit and bore,
The gay, the sad, the prurient and the prude ;
To count their total no one head is able,
E’en with a money-lender’s interest-table.
“How came that wealth?” Who wants to
know to-day ?
What though the owner is a rogue complete,
Who robs his clients—in a legal way,
Or, looking far too holy e’er to cheat,
Makes helpless women his especial prey,
Or sweats the sempstress —till she walks the
street,
Or starves : “ the money smells not,” says the
poet ;
So none should care to what ill-deeds they
owe it.
“A wealthy man !” what magic in the word !
The gilded cad is merely “ most outspoken ;”
The rich seducer—“ Well, perhaps he’s erred,
But hers the fault—her poverty’s the token ;”
A drunken beast—“Oh, yes, it lias occurred
That he the bounds of temperance has
broken.”
Be rich, and you may be a brute or devil ;
Be poor, you must not touch the skirts of evil.
And while these potent deities remain ;
While honesty and honor are as naught
To rank and wealth, that cover every stain
Of deeds ignoble by which they were sought ;
While wealth’s the test, and freeborn men will
deign
To truckle for distinction to a court ;
Our social system none can ever mend :
We’re fools, and suffer for it to the end.
WYVIS.

Hygiene teaches that nothing is so bene-
ficial to the human constitution as thorough
cleanliness, a theory verified by modern revela-
tions of the deadly microbes which infest the
flesh and blood—especially if we do not keep
ourselves in a clean condition, which ought to be
the first aim of every individual with any esteem
for himself. The use of Amykos has proved to be
very important to this end, by its great sanitary
power over the skin, mouth, teeth, &c. If you
value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration.*

My Dear Moorabinpa—
The reception given by Air. Clifford Hall<s and
Miss Ribbentrop, at the Grosvenor Hotel, the
other day, was one of the lamest shows in the
annals of daylight entertainment. A mere clutch
of people turned up, and sat round with the*
aspect of uninterested mourners waiting to hear
a dead insolvent’s will read, and listened to the
massive hostess devoting herself mostly to a friend
who had been quarantined with her. Aliss Rihhen-
trop waa so wrapped up in fighting her yellow-
flag battles oer again that I am sure the rest of
the audience felt rather de trap, which possibly
accounted for the early exodus.
Air. Halle is a tall, thin, adjutant-bird sort of a
man, reminding you of Captain Feildingand Ray-
mond Blathwayt rolled into one paste, and ap-
pears to suffer in private from a desire to lecture
Ids visitors. Doubtless Mr. Halle enunciated
many wise and improving statements in a long
afternoon of loquacity, for he is cruelly perfect in
his musical views, but most of his guests would
have preferred to hear either him or Aliss Rihben-
trop sing—an ounce of practice is worth a ton of
theory. However, neither “obliged the com-
pany,” which drifted away, wondering if Air. H.
and Aliss R. constituted a show in themselves,
and merely invited you to gaze upon them and
die, so to speak. As a rule, musical people give
you a taste of their quality on these occasions—
otherwise society can’t help marvelling why
they’ve roused it out 1
Lord Brassey has been feted in London previous
to his taking ship to Australia, and it has been
thought necessary to cable that “his lordship
would consider it his greatest duty to foster the
kindly feelings binding the silken fetters of mutual
pride and^ affection between Great Britain and her
colonies. I think this wild remark now comes
under the heading of “chestnuts,” and should
certainly be taken as written, since it is as integ-
ral a part of a Governor’s outfit as his cocked hat,
and people who solemnly and expensively wire it
out at every gubernatorial shuffle evidently havg
no idea of the value of cable-space.
But, desp'te many flattering indications of a
set-fair vice regal atmosphere for Alelhourne, I
have my doubts of Brassey’s success if the “good
story which a Sydney daily recently alleged
that Lord B. ‘ told about himself ” is a sample of
the new Vie. Governor’s abilities as a humorist.
I his is the “good story” (it certainly requires an
indicator or a hand-painting);—“ On the day pre-
vious his lordship spoke at Harrow at a meeting
in support of Dr. Barnardo’s homes. After the
meeting he started to walk back to London, and
came up to four little girls who had been at the
meeting. ‘ What did you think of Lord Brassey? ’
asked one. ‘I thought him a nice gentleman,’
said another. ‘I didn’t,’ said the third; ‘he
can’t speak a bit—he hummed and ha’d all the
time! ’ Apart from the awful revelation from
the lips of babes and sucklings that Victoria’s
new vice-regal ideal is one of those irritating after-
dinner orators who say that “ it—er—er—er— hum
affords me much—hum—pleasure to find myself
—er er hum,’ there is the deeper horror that
H.E. should consider such a Band of Hope anec-
dote worth repeating when the press was about.
Even Charley’s Aunt told a funnier tale than that.
Major-General and Airs. Hutton are not long
for this part of the world, as x the curly-haired
commandant’s term expires in April next, and
they will probably leave for ’Ome in that favor-
able month of exodus—Alarch. Golden-haired
Mrs. Hutton should receive a grateful send-off at
the hands of society, for her brilliant parties
stopped a very hungry hole during the late dreary
vice-regal administration. Looks, style, toilet,
tact and vyealth were all on her side, combined
with a pedigree almost as elongated as Hampden’s
and peppered with illustrious fighting-men, one of
whom is the Sir Richard Grenville immortalised
by Tennyson. The belligerent commander, how-
ever, is the son of a parson, though there must
have been a love of gunpowder in the family, for
Ins mother married a general after the cleric went
to head quarters with his report, as it were.
Talking of the milingtary, a warlike Sydney
military journal, evidently edited by a half-pay
captain with a flower in his buttonhole, recently
remarked : “We would like to see some fresh
blood infused into the Sydney Lancers. . . .
It seems strange that the merchant sons of Syd-
ney, who have all plenty of horses, should not
volunteer more to fill the ranks of the lancers.”
To my notion, it would he a good deal more
convincing if fresh blood were let out of the
pictorial Sydney Lancers, who have never got a
scratch during their nominally warlike career. A
soldier who hasn’t smelt gunpowder is as uncon-
vincing as his little tin prototypes in the toy-
shops. And, as for the “ merchant sons ” —the
corps was so outraged, once upon a time, at the
presence among it of a beastly tradesman who sold
socks, that Sydney youths with scales and a counter
even in the middle distance of their escutcheon
have ever since been too nervous to apply. They
never knew when this fastidious corps considered
that the drapah ceased and the Lanceah began.
Ami, apart from this, the expense of belonging to
the squad which bumps so at the trot is enough to
deter many a youth from entering. At least one
N.S. W. couple is absolutely piano-less owing to
hubby’s military ardor. The “century ”of piano
money went to buy a butterfly gold-laced suit for
Gussie—to go and be shot in by-and-by, for all he
or his wife knows to the contrary.
On dit that a recently- broken engagement
between a massively handsome Sydneygirl and her
late swain is almost on again. The lady recently
paid a visit to the native city of her former
Romeo, and report says a reconciliation was the
result. However, the re-engagement is not yet
officially announced, but society hopes for the
best.
The reception of honor to General Dodds (a
recent military meteor in the Australian social
firmament), at the Hotel Australia, last Saturday
evening, was a well-harbered, well-gloved spec-
tacle. with perhaps more English “poker” in
the mixture than is usual in French festivities.
But the, reception was inherently French, being
arranged by a French committee, with Dr. Laure
as president and M. Funck hon. secretary, and
including Messieurs Adet, Blanc, Durienx, Me-
tayer, Rev. P&re le Rennetel, and Dr. Rougier, all
of whom must have been charmed at the complete
success of their admirable arrangements, musical,
decorative, and otherwise. But until French com-
mittees learn to lay on a supply of national sparkle
for the use of their English guests only, they
cannot hope to preserve the delicate bouquet of
Parisian gaiety at their Franco-Anglo festivals.
As a regulator of spontaneous mirth, one average
English person balanced by three airy French
folks has the same effect as an ounce of sand to
three ounces of feathers. Even champagne-cup
a fa Dr. Monkhonse Brierly didn’t avail, on the
occasion I speak of, to bring the distinguished
company up to French soiree pitch.
The hero of the evening, General Dodds, who
twined additional laurels round his head during
the Dahomeyan war, came in great style, with a
gold-trimmed tunic leading gently on to a pair of
scarlet trousers and golden spurs. I thought those
spurs a good hint for the next Coolgardie lucky
digger. Or do they only suggest a new reading of
the “ silver spoon ” pryverb—“horn with golden
spurs on his heels ?” lam sure the General —who
was so gorgeously dressed himself that he must
appreciate fal-lalery—couldn’t help liking the
frocking, which was clever, taking the average,
and Frenchy into the bargain. The Acting-
Governoress (whose perpetual smile, by the
way, on occasions of this kind is creating a
dreadful smirk-record for the coming Viscountess
to live up to, especially if she happens to wear
shop-teeth) wore a black dress with a white front,
with plenty of glitter from jet and diamonds.
Aliss Darley (who always looks to me as if she
were sitting in judgment on the fleeting show)
also wore the hue of the minstrel corner-man’s
cheek. Mrs. Hutton was in grey and white satin
and diamonds. Mrs. (Justice) Innes glittered in
black, with violet-velvet sleeves, and a good dis-
play of the jewels Marguerite left her home for.
Aladame Lanre, wife of the president, also wore a
sparkling black gown—indeed, almost everybody
honored the occasion in her attire.
Perhaps- the most picturesque person in the
richly-dressed assemblage was a recent arrival
from France, who sported a long frock of white
brocade, with plenty of tail, anyhow, if the
bodice was only the size of a duty-stamp with a
bite out of it. She reminded me of the white girl
in the “ Peacemaker,’’ and was quite the opposite
of a lady in coooanut-ice combination, made in a
stage-ward robe sort of way —en polonaise, with
flights of little pink bows ; or a matron, with one
green feather on her head, reminding one of a
pretty Joey which had moulted all but its top-
knot. Alusic was amiably contributed by Madame
Kellermann—in yellow brocade, with a corsage
trimming of medals, a lady-amateur vocalist,
Kowalski, Poussard (always a personally-popnlar
artist), Vollrnar and Stael, while Madame Aengen-
heyster (in heliotrope) gave a French recitation.
The committee’s thanks to the trio of artistes took
the form of bouquets, but nobody thought of the
artists. Nobody ever does, though lam sure men
would appreciate a buttonhole just as much as
women gurgle over a bouquet.
East Sydney Rowing Club annual ball scored a
huge failure this year, when only a sprinkle of
couples turned up to play hide-and-seek in the big
The dressing was
quite as dispiriting as the attendance. One lady
had copied Flo. Young’s “ Madame Lange ” pink
and black dress as nearly as she could manage,
only she left out Aliss Young’s principal attrac-
tion—that shapely pink leg which shot out
through a crack in the draperies, now and again.
The rest seemed to be dressed in “art” costumes,
like bags gathered round the neck, though the
prevalent off-the-shonlder sleeve had a good fol-
lowing, of course. The E.S.R.O. have some idea
of cheapening down to a five-shilling dance next
year, I am told.
While talking about pay-to-go-in dances, the
annual ball in aid of Tempe Refuge (it’s just been
painted a deep cerulean bine—l mean the Refuge,
of course) is hilled for the 30th in Sydney Town
Hall, and means to wipe the flure with other
public dances of its weight and dignity. There
is to be fancy-dancing to amuse the galleryites,
but this will not interfere witli the public dancing
in any way—which almost looks as if the fancy
sets were going to perform on the ceiling. In
which case a crowded house is assured. The big
blazing Masonic ball occurs next evening (31st).
I think the idea of amalgamating all the little
Alasonio “socials ” into one aggravated entertain-
ment is a noble notion on somebody’s part.
Talking of festivities reminds me that the most
searching criticism it is possible to throw at any
social entertainment was thrown in Sydney a
while ago, after a high social clam-bake, or what-
ever it might be, was over, and the guests had
got away in apple-pie order, expressing themselves
as charmed and overwhelmed at the joy of spend-
ing such a pleasant evening until they got
round the corner. Then the whole crowd of
them (assorted species) bolted headlong fora cafe (I
don’t know which cafe, but it ought to have hi en
Paris House if they had ever studied the mouth-
watering menu which prevails, now thatLievain’s
taken the place in hand), and sat down to a large,
square, groaning supper—the whole hordeof them.
Then they really did begin to enjoy their evening.
Apropos of midnight suppers, I heard that one
large and merry group of 21 souls managed to
work up to the state of playing the piano with
their hats, and frivolities like that, on three
bottles of fizz! The miracle of the loaves and
fishes may now retire with its record broken.
“In Town ” seems to be the mustard-plaster of
the Gaiety Co.’s repertoire—l mean for drawing
houses to Sydney Lyceum, though it’s been
hustled in the straight, so to speak, by dainty
Decima Aloore’s vocal shakiness as Kitty Hether-
ington, Aliss Moore, of whom a very charming
picture by Falk is printed elsewhere in this issue,
however, atones for her little bronchial echo of a
sweet refined voice by putting real acting into it,
and making Kitty’s part something more than an
animated broomstick humbly waiting to give Cod-
dington his cues. Have you noticed that the bound
ingßradfield isn’t right up to date in at least one
hit of In’s pantomime in “ Some do it this way ?’’
Thesupposititious supper-scene, parexemple, when
the lady makes a great fuss tying the strings of
her “ dolman.” No woman ties her top wrap in
with a ribbon nowadays, so that pulley-hauling
business looks un-modern. The last time I saw
“In Town,” Claire Leighton took Aland Hobson’s
part in the tights of Romeo. I don’t like to be
personal, hut if Romeo’s legs were anything like
Claire Leighton’s, it’s no wonder he kept Juliet
firmly fixed at a great height above him when he
started the “ really mean it,” as the song says.
Apropos of the Gaiety girls—they remind me
that Sydney Johnniedom missed a rare chance for
a cheap quiz when they let the lithe and good-
looking Samoan dancing-girls (who are going to
do Europe on exhibit) escape per German steamer
in an unadmired state, and even missed attending
a large dance given in the copper-colored ladies’
honor, when there was, I hear, a dress (?) rehear-
sal of the Samoan ballets which are about to make
the Germans blush. The girls are said to be a
prime consignment—but when you come to think
of Mr. Louis Becke’s wicked stories “By Reef
and Palm,” it really seems quite providential
nobody put Gussie and Cholly near the track of
these potato-colored Yenuses.
In “Thoroughbred,” writes my Melb. corres*
pondent, Miss Faber wears the prettiest gown
she has shown up to date. Wall-paper pattern
brocade of pink azaleas on cream satin ground,
bodice of pale sulphur satin, shrouded with stem-
green accordeon chiffon. Miss Noble, too, looks
very bonny in her evening-gown of shell-pink
satin, empire style, swathed with chiffon and a
huge bunch of ragged scarlet poppies at her
breast; but she had no right to wear brown holland
and a schoolgirl white hat to the English Derby.
Despite the sneers of unsympathetic males, con*
tinues my correspondent, the Austral Salon is
booming. Last Monday, at Mrs. Saddler’s “at
home ” held at the Salon, the attendance was so
great that it bulged out of the door and kept the
lift-boy lively all the afternoon. There must have
been 300 women present and there were four men,
two of whom added to the gaiety of the afternoon
by singing dirgeful ballads. Lady Clarke, as
vice-president, has issued invitations for a conver-
sazione on Bth August, at Cliveden. The cards
bear the club badge, and all whose names appear
on the books as members or associates have been
bidden.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

The Dacres having left Sydney, after a
run of most undeserved ill luck, George Hignold
resumed business at Her Alajesty’s, on Saturday,
with “Youth.” A larger audience than usual
assembled to witness this spectacle, which started
at 7.45, and got to the end of its eighth act some-
where in the vicinity of midnight. There is more
drama for the money in “Youth” than in any
other spectacle now devastating this country; it
fills time and space, and hangs over into eternity
like a dry tree sticking out over a withered land.
Also, it is strictly virtuous, after a certain primi-
tive fashion. There are no fine shades of character
in it; the haughty villain is exceedingly wicked
and lofty, and the coarse villain is a hairy gorilla
with a black eye, and the hero is an individual of
oppressive purity, and there i*v,also an Irishman,
who is resolved to be funny of “bust.” Pre-
sumably, he undergoes the latter painful operation
after the audience is gone. The eight scenes are
all new and beautiful. There are 200 or more
British troops in the piece, and they tight man-
fully with a few niggers in a red desert, and fire off
800 guns, more or less; and G. Hignold, who is
supposed to have gone off for help, is suddenly
found standing in the centre with the limelight
on him, and a tattered banner in his hand. An
impression is gaining ground that Mr. Hignold
sleeps with the limelight on him. Another act
reveals a soul-stirring riot in a prison, with con-
vict G. Hignold rescuing the warder from an awful
death ; and a third great scene is the departure of
the huge troopship with Private Hignold in a
conspicuous place on the upper deck. The earlier
acts show the downward career of Lieutenant
Hignold, and the last one of all shows the return
home of ex-private Hignold, Y. 0., and the sudden
collapse of all his enemies.
• • •
The very numerous G. Hignold, above
mentioned does Ins part, such as it is, very well,
especially when he departs on the horse in search
of reinforcements, and suddenly returns with both
hands full of limelight and flag. Soot Ihglis, as
the polished villain, fills a stereotyped part
sufficiently well. The same brilliant observation
applies to Sweeney’s stereotyped Irishman, and
Miss Knights’ stereotyped adventuress, and the
stereotyped parson, and many other people. Mr.
Plimmer is notable as a pleasant and manly officer
conhealed behind an unusually shiny eyeglass, and
Miss Maud Williamson is another adventuress
who is a little less stereotyped, and whose bright
smile shows up nicely against the background of
Gaffer Hodge’s tombstone in Act I.
• • #
It is alleged that Mr. Rignokl’s next pro-
duction will be a great religious drama, “Joseph
of Canaan.” If this refers to the Old Testament
Imro, The Bulletin respectfully suggests that
Mr, Hignold should be Joseph; Mr. Scot Inglis,
Potiphar ; and Mr. Plimmer, Pharaoh. Also, it
proposes _ that Air. Sweeney should be Father
Jacob, with a powerful Irish accent.
** * *
“ In Town,” at Sydney Lyceum, has done
better business so far than the “ Gaiety Girl,’’and
could last for another fortnight if wanted, to
judge by present appearances. Sydney, in this
matter, has followed the lead of Melbourne, which
also returned “In Town ” at the head of the poll
by a large majority. The Bulletin doesn’t even
profess to know the reason of this public fervor,
but, having decided that Brad field is not a suf-
ficient explanation, it sets it down to the comic
back-view of little Fred Kaye, as the call-boy, and
leaves the matter at that. The “ Shop Girl ” and
“ Gentleman Joe” are to follow at some period in
the remote future.
** * *
The two variety shows at the Tivoli and
Empire are getting along reasonably well, con-
sidering that Parkes and G. Heid have lately been
furnishing all tile variety that any sane and
healthy person could ask for, and that nearly
100 other persons have been carrying on in a truly
dreadful fashion, gratis, all over the city and
suburbs. The spectacle of a hoarse candidate
gyrating on a public-house balcony by the glare of
a slush-lamp on a windy night, and wildly pro-
mising everything to everybody, while bags of
flour explode on his bat, is calculate to draw the
audience away from the best variety-show that
ever existed.

Hamilton’s Circus is living up to its early
reputation. The card-playing pony is just getting
into form, and the bauds that be holds are quite
dreadful to see; also, when it is his deal, he turns
up a big trump with fiendish malignity. Some
very clever child-gymnasts have been added to
the programme; also a wrestling bear, who lately
induced one of the audience to join in a rough-
and-tumble encounter, and got badly knocked
out. The general impression is, however, that
the bear had been stiffened by the bookmakers.

“ Thoroughbred, ” otherwise Roast Chicken,
galloped very short at Melbourne Princess’s, and,
after Alonday night, the invisible animal was
turned out to grass. The Bulletin would ad vise
B. and B. bo sell their phantom steed, without
reserve, to somebody who can run him at small
country meetings. In the language of sporting
journalism, he comes in baked after getting a
gruelling, and the Hoast Chicken pot boils over.
As a dead bird, he is no good, except, for laying
purposes. But enough of the mythical quad-
ruped, “Mrs. Tanqueray” was announced to
appear on Tuesday, and stay four nights. The
admirers of Aliss Olliffe include everybody who
has an eye for the picturesque or a soul to be
thrilled, therefore the few opportunities of behold
ing that charming actress m the character of
Paula should attract much attention.

On Saturday the B. and B. Co. will per-
form Hadron Chambers’ play “ John-a-Dreams,”
for the first time in the author’s native hemis-
phere. Half-a-mile of new scenery has been
painted for “John-a-Dreams,” and Chief Justice
Madden, having served the management with a
V. R. command, or writ, to produce the show, will
attend with his family and suite to see his orders
carried out. The arrival and departure of His
Excellency are to he indicated by the opening bars
of a certain unpopular melody, and the other bars
will remain open as usual between the acts,
