Harry Rickards took leave of Melb., at
the Opera House, on Monday, and is now crossing
the seas to engage new lots of variety artists. He
will be badly missed during his absence in foreign
parts. The boys have loved ’Arry for many
years, also their fathers loved him before they
were born, but he was never so generally doated
upon as now. The chorus of “’E dunno where ’e
are” trembled with emotion these last nights,
and its broken roar must have been audible in the
lower heavens. The great gap left by the retreat-
ing form of H. Rickards will be filled as much as
possible by Harry Dooley, who makes his first
appearance at the Opera House on Saturday.

A smoke-concert at the Melb. Bankers’
Institute last Thursday revealed the members of
a somewhat ’auglity calling in a genial light, and
was one of the best-arranged entertainments of the
kind that has happened to poor old Melbourne
since its deposits went into reconstruction.
Amateur talent expands under such treatment as
it receives from the elegantly-tailored bank
official in Ins social lair. Smoke-night perform-
ances seem to be improving steadily in quality.
Young Waxrnan’s imitations of Sass, Cecil Ward,
Tapley, Brough, Charles Norrie and Elton would
make a pretty big order for any professional
mimic to execute. A Liedertafel quintet party
are more than worth the listening to, although
they warble gratis, and the man with a yarn to
tell at a place where one is paid refreshments to
hear him is often a sound comedian. A novel
musical item on the Bankers’ programme was a
chorister-boy, who hit the sympathetic audience
on a tender spot with his young mezzo-soprano.
This was the same lad, Harold Fisher, who had
made a Cyril Tylerish success at the Town Hall
half-an-hour previously.
At Poverty Point,
Thus a very straighlspoken Bulletin correspondent—a
most competent critic, by the way—on Juliet Wray
Flegeltaub's) debut as Clairette with tbs W. and M.
Opera Co. at Adelaide. “With a handicap of ugly
features and no limbs or Aspire she simply brought down
the bouse, people in the stalls and dress-circle standing
up and shouting at the end of her song in the Hrst act
(‘Yes, I am the daughter of my mother,’ etc.). There
were two big encores and a third loudly demanded. A
novice who walks in and sweeps the floor with N Hie s
record as a vocalist is an artist even in this puritan city.
More ugly than otherwise, with a not too sympathetic
voice, she, by dint of a certain vim and magnetism,
achieved a triumph such as I've only heard once before,
and that was achieved by the refined and charming little
Deoima Moore in the same part. “Madame Angot ”
seems a lucky operetta to debut in. Flo. Young made
her biggest hit as Madame Lange.
A London correspondent paints the Dacres’ poor recep-
tion in Melb. as discouraging to other artists. But Aus-
tralia mirrors London, fair or unfair. Take the Gaiety,
on the other hand. In these dark days people will only
endure a show that amuses them. They will not stand
instruction, or moral lessons. They don’t want dramatic
beef and beer, but only oysters and champagne.

Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, Ah Gie, persists
in speaking of Miss Varley's “ secession ” from the W. and
M Opera Co. She was under engagement, to the Firm at
the time of her death, an engagement which would have
lasted until the middle of next year.—An Boon.
An Australienne, writing privately from London, says of
Melba’s singing in “ Faust “She lias a divine voice.
Each note is perfect, as perfect and pure as silence, if you
can understand. You can’t imagine her singing out of
tune, or fl it, or sharp, or harsh, and it rings through the
enormous Covent Garden, so that even when she is sing-
ing gotto voce you hear every note distinctly. Patti can’t
touch her, hut as an actress you simply can’t criticise her.
She doesn't act. I never saw a woman so devoid of ex-
pression and charm. Not a touch of magnetism. That
is what prevents her taking her right place at the very
top of the tree. In the dungeon scene, where she clasps
Faust and sings “ Oui,c’est toi I Je t’aime / ” she might
have been singing “That’s a pretty necktie.” Faust was
the handsome Alvarez. He took his top-note in the
garden-scene exquisitely and so fulfilled his mission. But
Plaocon ! Imagine a French Mephislopheles singing (in
his native tongue) like an angel, acting.like the very
devil, and pacing about like a panther in beautiful scarlet
tights (not that I ever saw a panther in tights). That's
Plancon, every lime. He is the best M. in the world. The
Blew de ior was superb. lam quite ashamed of myself
for not getting tired of Faust. 1 never shall.”
The show in which Lonnen and Alice Lethbridge were
shining lights went suddenly hung bef >re the last English
mail left. It seems that a new lady, Miss Ada Heeve, was
brought into rivalry with the Lethbridge, and awful ruc-
tions used to happen behind the scenes. Alice Leth. has
more than an average share of the jealousy which seems
inseparable from a business where success demands noisy
applause. She went into hysterics at Melb. Princess’s
when Mary Weir, for one memorable night only, broke a
promise not to dmce the serpentine whirl in fleshings.
Alice couldn’t display her own scraggy outlines, and
objected to the shapely Mary scoring in her stead.
Marshall Hall lias given publicity to adroll story against
a Melb. musical critic. Toe yarn has it that the news-
paper man, after hearing Madame Wiedermann sing the
same song twice at Halid’s concert the other Saturday,
went into Hie greenroom to enquire “ what the song
might be that madaine sang in response to her recall.”
People will note something more than an astounding joke
in this little incident. It is well for them to hear that a
free and independent “critic” is one who pays friendly
visits to the performers he is supposed to know only as
public artists,

An American damsel has adopted the toothsome stage-
name of Tartie Frightling. Not long ago a Gaiety girl
brought an action against a weekly journal in which she
had been casually described as a “ lari." The other little
charmer has no absurd scruples, evidently. We call her
little advisedly. She must he petite—little Tartie, and
real jam.
Contributions to the memorial to the late Lucy Cham-
bers—tlie most popular Australian singing teacher of her
time—have rolled in generously. It has been decided to
place a plain stone cross over Lucy’s resting-place, and to
devote the rest of the funds to found a memorial bed in
the Women’s Hospital. A most excellent way of keeping
the famous singer’s memory green.
Ada Crossley, it is said, would he quite disheartened by
her rehutfs but for Melba’s encouragement.
It is extremely plain, from certain recent Melb, events,
that no one who leaches music for a living should he
, allowed to act as musical critic tor the press. “No man
is a competent judge in iiis own cause ” —or in that of his
professional rivals.

Melba, on her meal-method : “I take about the same
thing every day in the year— cafi au lait, mutton-chops,
beefsteak, greens, fruit, and light wine, I never touch
cereals, bread, potatoes, pastry, or candy. I eat. fruit
three times a day. I only eat broiled meat, and I take it
with salad. I never drink anything cold, and as I don’t
fancy cooked water, 1 live on coffee, tea, and wine.”

Melb. Exhibition Building had to he taken. Also,
“Boucy ” intends trying some new lighting-effects of his
own devising.

Word comes from Bowen Downs Station
(0.Q.) that two attempts have been made within
three days to poison the shearers by
placing strychnine in their food. The first time

Sawyer, the Tory who last week called
John Burns a liar, and lost sundry teeth in the
scuffle, is a valet. Most British valets are Tories,
somehow. So, as a rule, are footmen, butlers—
and members of that club.
Sawyer will probably be pensioned by the Prim-
rose League.
The political revival of Lord Salisbury
inadvertently saved the Vic. Government a thou-
sand pounds. Hopetoun has accepted a nominal
billet in the new Ministry, and on the strength of
this hollow distinction he retired from his peculiar
position of Vic. Gov. on leave of absence and half-
pay. Satisfactory to know that Victoria’s gain is
not Great Britain's loss. Hopetoun’s seat in the
Cabinet has no salary hanging to it, but it is
sufficiently a sear to disqualify him from catrying
a scandalous farce any further. For the present,
and until Brassey swears in, Victoria has no
official deity, merely a ghostly imitation.
The Wild-Cat Column.
The Union Bank of Australia declares a divi-
dend for the half-year at the rate of 5 percent, per annum.
Last half-year the rale was C per cent., the one before it
was 8, in ’92 the rate was as hitch as 12 per cent.,
and seven years ago it stood at 14. The reserve-fund
Blands at the same figure (£1,000,000) as it did seven years
ago, the policy of the hank in recent years having been to
pay away practically all its earnings to shareholders. Con-
sequently, despite its acknowledged solidity, its dividends
have fallen heavily, and whether the end of the fall has
been reached is still uncertain.

That steady and methodical little concern, the
Ballarat Banking Co., has made a net profit of 4.3449,
against £3710 for the previous half-year, which, with
£28720n hand, makes £Bl2l available for distribution.
It pays its usual div. at the rale of 5J per cent, per
annum on its capital of £85,000 (£2337) and adds £l5OO
to reserve, which now stands at £50,000. The reserve, it
may’ be mentioned, lias increased by £9OOO since the
beginning of ’93. The Ballarat Bank lias cash and
Government debentures equal to 33 per cent, of its
liabilities—the best record among the purely Victorian
banks.

Colac (Vic.) shire council’s 30-year loan of
£lO,OOO at 5 per cent, was subscribed more than 10 times
over, and realised £lOB 3s. 9d., ihe A.M.P. Society gelling
the lot. Four banks and two in trance societies were
among the tenderers, and each ba k wanted to take the
whole £lO,OOO. The A.M.P. gels £4 7s. per cent, on its
investment, which, considering the security, shows that
the struggle for any kind of interest-paying stock is a
pretty hard one just now.

The system of accepting deposit-receipts (bought
on the open market) at their face value in payment of
bank calls is gradually “restoring confidence” in long-
da’ed deposits. Shareholders having calls to meet
naturally meet them with the paper which they can buy
at he biggest discount.

The N.S.W. Mont de about the steadiest
investment in the province, lias a net profit of £4239 (as
compared with £4470 last half-year), and £753 brought
forward from previous balance makes an available total of
£4992. The usual div. at 10 per cent, per annum absorbs
£3581 ; £5OO goes to reserve, making it £12,000 ; £l5O to
contingency fund to cover possible depreciation of securi-
ties, making it £6374; and £761 is carried forward.
Altogether the half-yearly report of the Mont de l’i6t<s
(which lives on adversity) is about the only sign of genuine
prosperity to he met with just now.

It used to be an axiom with Melbourne mer-
chants that no account should he drawn for until it
reached £2O. The writer saw, last week, one account of
£6 10s. that was drawn for in three hills at two, three,
and four months, and another of £8 12s. drawn for in four
bills at two, three, four, and five months.

The other day an endeavor was made to unload
in Melb. n consignment of strike-mc-dead cognac. The
official analyst said the stuff was worth about 6*l. a bottle,
and the shipment was destroyed—although the Customs
officials might have made a hit hy selling it to the N.S.W.
prison-authorities for medicinal purposes. The name or
the firm 10 which this extract of Gehenna was consigned
is religiously withheld by the authorities. The Abra-
hamses have discovered that it is a much safer game to
deal in adulterated liquor than in fictitious barrels. A
man’s reputation seems to keep better in spints.

• * * *
One Brown, a Melbourne accountant, giving
evidence before the Vic. Hanking Commission, stated
that £43,000,000 were lent out by the banks in Victoria,
and the average rate of interest was “at least 8 per
cent.” The banks get this money from the public at not
over 4 per cent, on an average, and lend it to the public
at double that price, and yet they can’t live on the dif-
ference. A State Land Bank, under even decent manage-
ment, could borrow at the same rate, and lend on
agricultural land up to half its value at 5 per cent., and
make about as much profit out of the transaction as the
private hanks do. It would not have to maintain 13
boards of directors, 13 boss managers, 13 big head offices,
13 different s'affs, and as many different sets of branch
offices with branch managers and staffs to match; it
would not require to advertise on the extensive scale the
present banks do in order to attract customers; and it
would not need to charge extia rates to make up for losses
on other classes of business, for it would not he driven
by competition to take risky business. The people who
borrow' from the private banks pay for all the 13
big head offices, and all the rest of the gorgeousness, and
the bad debts, and the advertising, and Hie oilier circum-
stances. Brown makes the eminently sensible suggestion
that the State should take over from the hanks all their
agricultural inorlages, and become responsible for an
equal amount of their deposits, and thus start the Slate
Land Bank right off. Then it could, in consideration of
the extra security afforded, convert the deposits into
Jong-dated 3.J per cents., and, in the case of depositors
who wouldn’t convert, it could pay them off and get new
ones in their stead. With the saving in interest and
working expenses it could reduce mortgage interest im-
mediately to 6 per cent, all round and yet leave a large
margin to cover any losses through had business taken
over, and when the bad business was nil liquidated it
con’d make a further reduction to 5 per cent, without
difficulty. This is about the most practical and business-
like idea up to date.

John Howell comes back to N.S.W. as manager
of the Australian Smelting Co., which is going to try and
s dve the Broken Hill sulphide problem by the new
Siemens and Halske process, and w ill establish works at
Lake Illawarra. The co.’s capital will be £600,000.
Special arrangements are said to have been made with
the Junction and Proprietary, and the process is stated
to cost under £4 per ton for zinc-blende orei.

ills secret. Arrangements are proceeding for the erection

The North mine (8.H.) has changed hands for
the fifth time. The only people who have made money
out of it are those who hare bought it to sell. The
original co. was in 80,000 £1 shares ; the New North had
£l shares. The latter erected expensive but
lumbering coimentratiiig works to deal with ttie car-
bonates, but s mt them down, and long ago the
mill was partly dismantled. Apart from a few patches
of rich cupriferous carbonates, got at 200 ft., little has
been exposed in the mine Iml low-grade sulphides and
some very low-grade carbonates. At 300 ft. they pro-
spected most extensively, but most unsuccessfully ;
though there is still some ground W'est to fossick in. The
cute “ South crowd ” is, however, now standing by the
new venture, which has a nominal capital of £lOO,OOO,
and proposes to see what can be done with Hie sulphides.

“I went out lately to see a mine a good many
miles from Coolgardie, and would not have exchanged
nr bools for the show. A few days ago it was sold for
£7OOO. Another riiiu J was put under offer to a middle-
man tor £4500. He brings out a buyer who approves at
£7OOO, pays £750 deposit, £3OO of which goes to Mr.
Middleman, who, on completion of sale, drew £2200
further commission. You cannot approach anyone about

plunder.

• ♦ * *
“Jack” Lyall, the well-known legal manager
of Launceston, has just been prosecuted in the local
police-court on a charge of forging a transfer of scrip In
the West Volunteer P.A., which, until lately, he managed.
The charge arises out of the recent West V. scandal in
which the cutting of the lode with paying stone was
“ hushed ” for a few days to the benefit of the few in the
“know.” A few shares were distributed amongst em-
ployes at the mine—it has byen.said for hush purpose—
and quite recently one of them applied to the Supreme
Court for a rectification of tire register on discovering
that his name was not there. It then transpired that the
shares given him had been transferred by a foreign signa-
ture purporting to he Ids, and that he had not received
any of the divs. declared during his shareholding. Re-
sult—the recent prosecution eventuating in Lyall's com-
mittal for trial. For the defence it is urged that the
shares were only given to enable donee to attend and
vote at a particular meeting, and were re-transferred by
his purported signature—that such proceedings are usual
in such cases and, in the present instance, were, like Vic.
ex-members’ use of defunct passes “ without intent to
defraud.”

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It h as long been known to scientists that for
Improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency tf
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted t<
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion cf
peach and ivory may be had by anyone--who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Mr. Justice Innes, of N.S.W, Supreme
Court, a good lawyer and dispassionate Judge, has
had health, and finds himself compelled to take
six months’ leave of absence. This, if needed,
will no doubt be extended to a year, at the end of
which his Honor (who will then have served
15 years) may, if he chooses, retire on pension.
The N.S.W. public sympathises with Judge Innes,
and cordially hopes that he will soon return to
the Bench “as good as in w.”
Old Geoi’ge Bates
(born in 1800 at Old-
street, St. Luke’s, Lon-
don) still lives, hale and
hearty, in Adelaide Des-
titute Asylum. He landed
on Kangaroo Island in
1824 and lived there al-
most continuously till
removed to the city last
year. He was on the
mainland with the natives
in 1827, and four years
later he helped in the
search for Capt. Barker,
who was killed at the
Murray mouth, and in
memory of whom a mural
slab was erected at the
time In St. James’ Church,
Sydney.
N.S. W ales Premier
Reid, at a big meeting
last week, said he had
everywhere been received
with such enthusiasm that
he would have to take
care his head didn’t swell.
In retiring from that very
meeting he wished his
audience good-night, and
added, fervently, “ God
bless you ! ” When actor
Elliston produced a mimic
coronation at Drury Lane
just after the accession of
Geo. IV., Elliston him-
self impersonated the
Sacred Majesty, and, in-
toxicated with applause,
actually came to believe
himself a real King.
“ Bless you, my people ! ”
he exclaimed. So far,
Georgey-Porgey, of N.S.
Wales, has only got to
“ God-bless-ing ” the elec-
tors. This week he’ll
probably be leaving out
God, and blessing “my
people” on his own
hook.

Somebody once asked N.S.W. boss Orange-
man Jawbone Neild whether he’d take a little
Benedictine. ‘ ‘Never drink it,” replied Jaw-
bone. “ Because it is made by Catholic monks ?”
enquired his host. “ Oh, no—l like Chartreuse,
which is also made by monks—though when I
have a choice between yellow Chartreuse and
green I always take yellow.’’ Making the best of
■both worlds.

Premier Reid says the N.S.W. public
won’t take much notice of Wise until the latter
becomes as fat as he (Reid) is. There’s a lot in
this. The besotted public really think a man
knows nothing until he is bald and fat—or

News ’ artist Longstaff, lately returned from
Europe. A number of the residents of the Valley

William Tarleton, the tedious veteran
Hobart B.M. who dropped dead in the street
whilst returning from church, was 75 years of age,
and had been on the bench since his arrival in Tas-
mania in 1842. As Assistant-B.M. he sat, for a
time, in company with the notorious John Brice-
then B.M. at Hobart. Tarleton, though a
strict disciplinarian, in the early days did much
towards lightening many of the tyrannies of the
system.
One of Williamson and Musgrove’s living-
picture models has, The Bulletin hears, just
become the spouse of a 22-year-old Melb. johnny.
The lady, a native of the Southern city, will lie
remembered as having outshone in face and form
all the remaining poseuses of the show, both im-
ported and home-made. The bridegroom is the
heir of one of the oldest (white) Vic. families, and
his possessions, transmuted into ready cash, would
probably run into five figures several times over,
even at present values.

Mr. J. W. DICKINSON.
Mr. J. W. Dickinson, runner-rip to Mr.
Coni on in the N.S.W. bowling championship
matches, proven his quality by beating .39 ardent
competitors. He is a native of Sydney, andl
stands in the first rank of Australian bowlers.
He played for the Glebe in ’94 in the first cham-
pion rink (against all clubs), and materially con-
tributed to his team’s victory. He lias been a
most successful captain in matches between
N.S. W. and Victoria and Maoriland; and was
playing for the Glebe club when it won, three
years in succession, the “John Young trophy.’’
He also won the intercolonial championship
medal against Victoria. Mr. Dickinson is Mr,
Conlon’s brother-in-law, so there must surely be
a favorable bias in bowling relationship.
A recent Australasian contained a poem
by Adam Lindsay Gordon, entitled “The Feud.’’
It was given as previously unpublished, but editor
Bonython, of Adelaide Observer, printed it in a
London literary paper some years ago. The Aus-
tralasian editor expressed doubt as to whether it
is a genuine Gordon relic or not. The doubt
was unnecessary. It was written by Gordon at
Mt. Gambler in 1863, shortly after the publication
of the pictures of Baton (subsequently Sir Noel
Baton), one of which formed its subject, and was
the first original composition of any length pro-
duced by the Centaur poet. It was thought so
well of by his friends that, sorns time afterwards,
he had 50 copies of it printed at the office of the
Border Watch, in Albury, for private circula-
tion. Gordon did not, however, think it of
sufficient merit to be included in his published,
poems. And his judgment was correct.

The Abrahams brothers, lately convicted
of fraudulent trading by a Melbourne jury, are
said to have made thousands of pounds in their
time out of legitimate transactions with the Vic.
Government. Although the business was scan-
dalous, the discredit of it rests upon the other
people iu this instance. The Abrahams firm used
to buy up from the Vic. Defence Dept, all the
“ obsolete ” rifles discarded by the local army, and
sell them again in England at a big profit. Each
time the arms of the precious forces were brought
up to date the old weapons were disposed of tor
“a song ” to the local jobbers, instead of being
shipped, at a fair market price, to a foreign gun-
factory.
One “J. C. Rounding” points out, in
Sydney Evening News, that Britain, which is a
Ereetrade country, lias largely reduced its public
debt, while France, Germany, Austria, Russia
and Italy, all ot which are heavily protected, are
piling up their State liabilities alarmingly. Ihe
other side of the story is that Britain paid on its
debts with almost the same energy while it was a
protected country ; that the United States, which
is also protected, lias reduced its liabilities even
faster than Britain ; while Turkey and sundry
Spanish-American republics have practised Iree-
tiade, and have piled up big debts and then gone
broke. And neither set of tacts proves anything,
after all.

Dear Bulletin, —Be Michael Davitt's con-
tention that fear of capital punishment deters
only those nervous people who do not need deter-
rence, the nervously-constituted man-impelled
by an emotional storm to a violent act is un-
questionably much more oblivious of consequences
than the cold-blooded contemplator of murder,
who would, therefore, most experience whatever
deterrent effect there might be in the prospect of
retributorv killing. Nervousness, in any case, is
so carefully discriminated by psychologists from
cowardice that Lewes calls it “in many instances
merely the reflex index of the temperament which
is capable, upon emergency, of the highest courage
of all.”— Speculum.
Some of the Victorian importers now feel
very sorry they spoke in the matter of locally-
manufactured shoddy. If the proposal that all
goods fie stamped according to their quality, under
severe penalties for deception, be carried, it will
matter comparatively little so far as local goods
are concerned, seeing that the Vic. factories are
within reach of the law and can be kept up to the
mark. But the English and German shoddy-
makers being out of reach, the responsibility of
their deceptions will rest on the importer, who is
the man within gunshot range of the statutes.
Moreover, if the law is enforced, even reasonably
well, there will be such an awful exposure of the
import shoddy business as was never heard of
before. And the intention of the importers was
simply to expose the local makers ; the idea of
being exposed themselves is altogether new.

on. We 011I3’ wish some means could be devised of killing
the root of such measures, so that they should never again
spring: up to taint the political atmosphere with a foul
flavor of disloyalty and imperial disintegration. Instead
of desiring to see our powers of self-Government extended
by the right to elect a Governor in the colony, we would
prefer to s i, e the whole too elaborate machinery of Con-
stitutional Government swept away and the powers of
real Government entrusted to the hands of a competent
Imperially-appointed Governor. The advent of_ an elec-
tive Governor would be the signal for all who desire to live
under a system of Government guaranteeing the security
of life and property to clear out of Republican New
Zealand.
The “foul flavor of Imperial disintegration” is
especially good.

liam Wilson has stolen Mr. Thomas Ran-
dolph's lugger. He was a diver of this
: boat, and went with It to the pearling sta-
. ticn on Sunday, and made several attempts

' forcibly conveyed her in a boat to the lug-
ger, and then set sail in the direction of
Maladugn. Another boat gave chase, but
carried away her foresail, and had to rec-
tum.

Society
Prior Vaughan kept very quiet in Sydney;
all the same, lie canvassed most persistently and
successfully on behalf of his St. Gregorian scheme.
He is said to have received some large amounts
incognito from donors whose names, if revealed,
would cause a sensation.
* * *
Womanhood Suffrage does not exist in
N.S. W., yet at the ’94 election one member
of “the other sex” recorded her vote—at one of
the outside polling-places of Glen Innes electorate.
Her husband was ill, so she took his “right” and
marched bravely off to the ballot-box. The poll-
clerk at first refused to accept the vote, but
madame argued him into believing that, as the
husband couldn’t attend, she was perfectly eligibl;
to represent him. The upshot was that member
Wright got the benefit of the only female vote yet
taken in the province
* * #
The Vic. Acting-Governoress is a potentate
to suit the times. Ever so many melancholj
dressmakers wrote to her to say their trade was
languishing for want of encouragement at head-
quarters. So she devised a fancy-dress ball for
next month at which it is imperative that every-
one shall wear fancy clothes. She has had many
letters from designers, costumieres, &c., saying
that the idea has not only helped them but let
them employ a large number of out-of-work
seamstresses.
* • •
Dear Bulletin, —The dead set now being
made in Melbourne against the Bicycle Woman
is, I think, to be explained by the Queen of Spain
legend. Right-thinking persons consider it inde-
cent for a woman (off the stage) to make public
the fact that she has legs. There is a fortune
waiting for the man who will invent a side-saddle
bicycle. The New Woman will not, of course,
patronise it, her ardent desire being to evolve legs.
By the way, is it not curious that, in spite of
physiology, man always gives his sculptured or
painted ideal wom«n small-r heads and longer legs
than the average natural woman possesses? The
Venus de Medici has the longest body and short-
est legs of all the Greek female statues —and even
then they are longer than those of the modern
average female. It cannot, I think, be impressed
upon woman too strongly that she is an absurdly
short-legged animal, and that her salvation lies in
skirts.— Creevk Roe.
* * •
Dear Bulletin, —He is the small imper-
tinent son of a near relative, and is allowed Aus-
tralian liberties. He chips in the conversation of
the eldors and occasionally enlightens them. Frin-
stance : we were discussing the eternal female as
now manifested, and deploring her scramble for
the pants. “My word, she wouldn’t want ’em if
she went to our school,” said the small boy. and
he sat very feelingly on the extreme edge of his
chair and sighed. There is a soul of good in things
evil—even skirts. — Snell.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, — I saw the New Woman
15 years ago in the Tumut Ranges. She was
carrying a heavy swag, and her husband, who
followed her, carried a pipe in his mouth. I asked
them to take some billy-tea I was making at the
time. Both sit down. The man said, “ Chuck
over that swag,” The woman chucked it, and he
placed it under his head, and, stretching himself
out at full length, remarked, “Now, mate, I call
this comfortable.” The New Woman of these
present years doesn’t seem to know what a rod she
is putting in pickle for herself, —C.R.

Lady Hopetoun’s cousin, Mr.
lias become engaged to a daughter of the late
Salmon Wilson ; almost simultaneously with thC
promulgation of this announcement comes the
news that, under their deceased father’s will, the
Wilson girls get .£50,000 each. Miss Wilson (Mr.
Ralston’s hride-elect) is .a pale, somewhat diminu-
tive damsel, demurely “English’’ in deportment
and dress, and with so little love for things Aus-
tralian that she only consented to the recent
Ercildonne sojourn for health reasons.
* *
The traditional coolness between th°
Hopetouns and a certain tomboy heiress is said
to have originated at one of the first up-country
meets patronised by their Excellencies. To beguile
the preliminary wait, the frisky damsel in question
joined the men of the house-party in a friendly
game of leap-frog on the verandah, to the intense
enjoyment of all concerned. When the fun was
at its height, the Countess suddenly quitted her
post of observation, and, .addressing her hostess in
icy tones, requested to be driven to the station as
she wished to go home. And home she went, the
prayers and tears of the assemblage notwith-
standing. Lady Hopetoun never forgot this
episode, and in after years when a polo hall was
being arranged in the vicinity of her favorite
summer residence, she warned the invitation com-
mittee to choose between herself and the dashing
culprit aforesaid.
The cable announces that Gov. Brassey
and his wife are “eager to give their hearts’ pros-
perity and happiness for the elevation of the
people.” Which means just as much and as little
as if he said that he was prepared to saorifice the
hereditary perspective of his liver in order to pro-
mote the habit of making clear soup out of gum-
trees. Is there no such thing in existence as a
plain, sensible Governor, who won’t make a fool
of himself by waving a maudlin and incoherent
fork at banquets, but will simply state that he is
going to draw his salary regularly, and sign all
needful State documents in a legible hand, and
make himself as agreeable as circumstances will
permit ?
* * •
A Melb. ladies' school got up some scenes
from Shakespeare in aid of a local charity, and
the manageress stipulated that not a single ticket
should be sold to an unmarried man. One im-
posing pater-familias represented the male element
in the audience. All went well but dismally till
the stage-director, for the purposes of illusion,
began to burn a pale-blue, harmless light—as he
thought. It turned out to be a strong compound
of sulphur. In a moment the stage was blotted
out, and the crowd of ladies, all in full evening-
dress and in closely-packed rows, found them-
selves in a thick, yellow-black cloud. Strug-
gling, spluttering, thinking they were all
going to be burnt -to death, they fell over
one another pell-mell, tearing each other’s
dresses, and getting mixed up in each
other’s ornamental combs, as th y fought their
way out of doors. Then the cry went up from
the hearts of all the pretty school damsels— “ Oh
for a man ! ’ For paterfamilias had been the first
to bundle out of the ball door, and the stage-
director, in order to make him look as much like a
woman as possible, had been swathed in silk from
top to toe and fastened up with safety-pins. He
had to be dragged out bodily by the kitchen-maids
to prevent him from suffocating in the cause of
decorum. Not one of the crowd of women present
had thought of throwing up the windows.
# # 9
Shipping item from Queensland paper :
“ The steamer Oiten arrived to-day at Thursday
Island with lepers for the Torres Straits leper
station.” Evidently the time is coming, or has
already come, when a steamer-load of lepers will
be regarded in the same matter-of-fact light as a
steamer-load pi frozen meat. "By-and-bye, special
freights and insurance rates will lie quoted for this
kind of cargo, and vessels will be lost through a
quantity of bulk lepers shifting during a gale and
putting the vessel on her beam-ends, or
through overloading, or spontaneous combus-
tion, or through want of sufficient allowance
for the possible swelling of the lepers cn
route. The Bulletin can see with a prophetic
eye the manifest of some tramp steamer on
the N.Q. coast ten years lienee; Ten tons
common or inferior lepers shipped as dead
weight; 40 crates of rare lepers with peculiar
symptoms, very valuable as medical curiosities;
quantity of refrigerated leper; one elephantiasis
patient in loose box on main deck; one insane
leper confined in hen-coop; stuffed leper in case
for museum ; petrified leper shipped as geological
specimen; one cask of pickled leper in brine ;
tombstone for prominent leper lately deceased ;
missionary; one box of hymn-books for leper-
station ; suspected leper in second cabin going up
for medical examination.
# * *
The leper-boy, Molloy, froin Rockhampton
(Q.), has been pronounced no leper on bacterio-
logical examination of his blood. He now seems
to be recovering. His case was pronounced true
leprosy by eight doctors. Nor can the leprosy
bacillus be found in the blood of the Kanaka,
“Bob,” also getting better. What price the
leprosy-judgment of the average medico ? About
lid., The Bulletin guesses.

• * #
Extract front a speech by that wild Irish-
man, Chief-Justice Madden, of Vic. :
It was characteristic of the English people that, much
as they admired high courage, they were disposed to
insist that it should not he accompanied by anything
like dramatic effect. ... It was the cool, calm
determination to perform duty a id look for no further
reward which had made England's glorv wherever she
had been called upon to exhibit this quality. ... It
was this quality which won for England the battle of
Albuera, where a handful of Englishmen held “the hill
against a great mass of the,enemy, who supposed, from
the confident attitude of the men, that they were sup-
ported by strong reserves. The result was that the
French retreited, leaving the hill in the hands of IheEng-
lish. At Alma the day was won a dozen times by the
Russian army, hut the devoted bravery of small detached
bodies of English soldiers overthrew the opposing columns
which numbered at least 10,000.
Then, having done a final whoop about England,
Madden proceeded to distribute the Humane
Society’s medals to a large mtmher of people,
named Murphy and MacTavish and Muldoon and
O’Brien and Donald Mac Knight and Franz Oscar,
and things like that, who.had distinguished them-
selves by acts of valor.-

The Adelaide doctors have been boy-
cotting Nurses Hawkins and Graham, whose
vigorous protests against the promotion of Miss
Gordon, the sister of the Chief Secretary, cause A
the Hospital trouble in the city and their own dis-
missal. A Royal Oommission ordered their re-
instatement, but the Board declines to act upon
the recommendation. Premier Kingston has
announced his determination to enforce justice
for the two ladies, and at present an awkward
deadlock continues.

The Saturday Review (June Ist) :
When Fowell Buxton was appointed to the
Governorship of South Australia, it was felt that the
Imperial authorities had afforded the colonists the
strongest possible excuse for demanding the right to
choose in future their own Governors.
• #* •
The Vic. Crown Law officials are in posses-
sion of full details as to the instigators of a re-
cent attempted “embracery.” The individual
who was put away had been led to expect that
somebody would be provided for whilst he was
“doing time,’’ but a breach of faith in that respect
has caused him to take revenge by telling what he
knows.
• • 9
A fresh young English lady came to Mel-
bourne 18 months ago and became a nurse in one
of the Melb. hospitals. Whilst washing a patient
who suffered from a loathsome disease, she became
inoculated through a scratched finger, and died
in great pain within a fortnight.

Let us federate ! The oldest London
.llustrated weekly gives a picture of the newly-
appointed S.A. Governor as “Governor of Wes-
tern Australia, now known as Westralia ” (a
Bulletin coinage, by the way). A few months
back, another London illustrated with an Aus-

Robert Duff, Governor, Victoria, N.S.W.”
• # #
The Queensland planters, having robbed
the South Sea islands to the utmost limit, are
now proceeding to rob one another, tip North,
agents, either white or Mack, are sent out from a
plantation, furnished with the fullest information
as to likely recruiting-grounds. They visit the
different plantations and talk with the boys whose
time has expired, or is about to expire. After
finding out the rate of wages they receive, the
agent, on behalf of his superior, offers a higher one.
and thus induces hands to leave their employers
in the lurch at a critical time. The morals of
nigger-driving !
• * #
A rise in N.S.W. horse-hair ! With Judge
Stephen in England, Judge Lines on six months’
leave. Judge Windeyer expecting a Privy Council
call ’Ome (which he won’t get—if the N.S.W.
taxpayer has to stand the racket) and Chief
Justice Darley engaged in gubernatorial functions,
there is a flutter in the hawkeries of Sydney.
In its criminal jurisdiction the court is very
short-handed. Chief Justice D irley, Acting-
Governor, cannot sit; Judge Windeyer, in view
of recent events, will not, or (in the opinion of
most people) should not sit; Judges Inims and
M. H. Stephen are ill; Judge Owen is in Equity,
and never wears scarlet and ermine; Judge
Manning (Bankruptcy) is, to the great regret
of all who have practised before him, in very bad
health, and seldom takes a hand in criminal
matters. So that, the whole of the work has been
threatening to fall on Mr. Justice Simpson. It
looks as if Premier-Barrister Reid would have
some nice morsels of legal patronage to dispense.
In Judge Lines’ absence, Mr. Henry E. Cohen,
erstwhile Minister of Justice, will have a seat on
the Supreme Court bench. Mr. Cohen is a man
of much ability as a commercial lawyer, but his
appointment came rather as a surprise to the public
—some of whom expected to see the senior Dis-
trict Court Judge (Murray) temporarily appointed.
The Ministry which inaugurates a system of ap-
pointing to the Supreme Court comparatively
young men of experience and vigor will deserve
public gratitude.
• * #
“ Canteen ” obtains at Trial Bay (N.S.W.)
gaol, where the confinees are allowed to expend
one third of their earnings in tobacco, newspapers,
dainty foods, &c. This system has proved more
th in successful, and is seldom abused. Trial Bay
3 the Eden of the law-breaker, with its daily
rage, “canteen,’’ easy supervision, and compara-
tive freedom from restraint, and its privileges are
■>ften extended to the undeserving. For example,
»ne man now there has spent 42 years in prison',
while others are inmates for the fourth and fifth
time.

A Vic. civic official, lately awarded a year
for embezzlement, received a salary of exactly ss,
63d. per day-though he annually handled
.£14,000.
• • •
Bananaland legislators are suppressing
gambling. In deference, however, to the
“ snufflehusting ” vote, which will be much in
requisition next year, exceptions have been made
in favor of bazaar-raffles, church-consultations,
&o. Which reminds one of a certain Northern
celestial, who peddles lottery-tickets at the
bottom of a leprous, unclean fruit-basket. Ap-
parently he goes free, like the parsons.

The officers of Vic. Cadet Force have long
agitated for the privilege of wearing a more gor-
geous uniform. They submitted colored plans of
the sartorial magnificence their martial souls hun-
gered for, and as they were prepared to pay for
the new upholstering, the Government has
graciously acquiesced :
Murder is the soldier’s trade,
And, catch him where you will
His greatest effort’s alwaj's made
In dressing up to kill.
• # •
The depression in Melbourne has created
a demand fot cheap coffins, and it has been met by
a local genius with a lath-and-plaster corpse-con-
tainer which is going off well, the undertakers
say, and giving pleasure to all. The new coffin is
fire and burglar proof and impervious to damp,
and the makers protest that persons who have
once tried their coffins never use any other. Bury-
ing is now so cheap in Melbourne that it scarcely
pays to be alive.
# # *
A Sydney parson, lately transplanted to
the South, has sent bis church-choir into red
rebellion. The choir —a voluntary one having
been “ let off ” the collection for the last 40 years
(mure or less), sent up a. wild howl some Sundays
hack when the plate was passed up, which said
church-requisite has been presented regularly ever
since, and as regularly sent back sans contribu-
tion. Besides which, the shepherd has made
trouble with his flock over other financial matters,

The Rev. Wade, of Melbourne, loudly
deplores the fact that modern men live too fast,
and beseeches them to slow down. On the con-
trary, the modern man advises the parsons to
catch up.

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos
the recent Yio. shock of four
children at a birth. I lay odds
that the parents are each one of
twins. It is a cert, that twin
blood is on one side of the
parentage. I’ve had a deal of
experience in breeding stock,
principally sheep, which gener-
ally produce one at a time—
hence the human analogy.
Now, when twins were dropped,
I always had them put with
their mothers in a special pad-
dock, after carefully marking
them; and when matured I
mated them. A twin ram
mated with ordinary ewes very
frequently got twins, and a
twin ewe mated with an ordin-
ary ram also produced twins
very regularly. Going further,
1 mated a male twin with a
female twin, and in several
cases got triplets and quar-
tettes as the result. These in
turn, when mated, produced
wonderful results, but in every
case the progeny were still-
born. What I wish to point
out is, that twins should not
marry twins. The risk is too
great. Unfortunately, I myself
am one of triplets, and the gtrl
I adore is one of twins. I dare
not marry her.—Old Jackkeoo.
# # *
It is common in the North
to hale a blackboy or gin before
the magistrate, and induce the
aboriginal to put his or her
mark to a so-called agreement
binding the said nigger to serve
for several years. This passes
for a legal contract, but it is
really unadulterated slavery.
Another aboriginal grievance :
some lady down South fancies
she would like to have a black
picaninny to attend upon her,
and gives the order to an agent. The picaninny
can generally be procured, for there are several
picaninny-purveyors in the'North.

A lady lately died up North, leaving be-
hind her an indifferent husband and a broken-
hearted lover (who had worshipped her in her
pre-matrimonial days). Sympathy, strange to
say, went, not to the husband, but to the other
man ! It was the case of the cruelly-treated wife
and the old (and generally generous) adorer.
“Yes,” said a lady friend, “we ought to pity
Mr. Blank. I suppose wo’vo "ot to regard him
as the ‘ illegitimate widower 1 ’ ”
# * #
On the very Melbourne beat where the
big jewellery robbery took place, the other week,
a constable was reprimanded for being a minute
late in meeting his inspector at a given spot. A
constable told the writer that it is impossible to
convince some inspectors that being late may, in
some cases, be due to watching a suspicious
character, and that in consequence constables
prefer to let such matters slide rather than risk
being reported and perhaps fined for being behind
time. And yet the authorities are amazed that so
many burglaries occur. Burglars pick their time
to the minute, and are always safe from disturb-
ance so long as this system of meeting inspectors
at fixed spots at fixed times is in force.
m * *
With reference to a paragraph which ap-
peared in The Bulletin of 22ud June, stating
that a memorial brass-plate had been removed
from St. Mary’s Cathedral, and giving probable
reasons for the act, this paper is informed on the
best authority that the plate referred to was
merely taken down during the renovation of the
altar, and will be immediately replaced in a better
and more suitable position in the cathedral. As
there is not a shadow of truth in the insinuations
contained in the paragraph, The Bulletin very
much regrets its insertion.

The result of the latest Melb. bogus
criminal-assault case keenly satirises President
Rogers’ dictum, in the Dean Commission report,
about the unlikeliness of “a young girl on Hie
threshold of life” doing desperate things. The
Bulletin wishes the Melb. Women’s Christian
Association, or whatever they call themselves, joy
of the charming young lady whose “ future ” they
were so benevolently guarding. Also, Mr. Cham-
pion’s usual luck still sticks to him.
* * •
“ Visiting a township after an absence of
20 years,” writes a commercial traveller to The
Bulletin, “I put up at the old coaching hotel,
and found the whilom groom and cook had joined
forces and were the proprietors. The railway had
deposed King Cobb, and the departure of the
drummers had shorn the house of its ancient
glories. On going upstairs I met the proprietress
opposite the bath-room, and had my attention
duly called to it, and to the advantages of the pre-
sent water-supply over the tank and water-cart of
the old time. ‘Ye know that (meaning the bath)
saves the girl dragging water upstairs and the
slops down, for she can empty them in the bath.
An’ it’s so handy when the water’s turned off ;
why, the other day it was cut off at five minutes’
notice, and we filled the bath and kept the house
going with it, as we sold the tanks to the selectors
when the waterworks were finished.’ Water from
that bath for culinary and drinking purposes !
Ugh !”

Because a London clergyman devotes bis
evangelising energies entirely to the lost sheep of
tiie flesh-colored-tights-and-high-kicking profes-
sion, a call has been made in cold print for
some Australian city pastors to do likewise. It
shouldn’t be difficult to answer that call. Any
merely tepid-blooded parson should be warm-
veined enough to volunteer to form Bible-classes
and prayer-soirees with (say) Sydney Lyceum
“ Gaiety ” girls. “ The intemperate kick of ouah
deah sistali Madge Rossell,” for instance, might
be made a frequent subject of special prayer.
Lessons in angelic leering, so as to spiritualise the
sensuous smile of sistah Grace Palotta, would be a
most encouraging branch of missionary work.
All this is zeal-firing enough, but there remains
beyond it a large and arid field for which,

going to tackle the conversion of the ungodly
newspaper - man ? Of the pagan priest who
exalteth the horn of the Gaiety Girl, so to speak ?
Of the false prophet who maketh the common and

henna’d tints? Even General Booth, whose nose
oidy would almost pawnbroke Paradise without
any collateral security, is said to have despaired of
converting reporters. The writer has known one
or two converted reporters, but they couldn’t
report worth a cent, and their only recommenda-
tion was that they could always be found when
anything unpleasant had to be “represented ” by
“the paper.” There is, therefore, a loud call for
a New Evangelist who will devote his life, his
pastorhood (and his purse, if it’s worth it), to the
ungodly newsnaper-man.

Dear Bulletin, —To those abandoned
miscreants who, like myself, combine freecj|i)tn *
from any theological brand with an intimate
knowledge of the “pore heathen,” there is a
noble feast of farce in the figures with which
Romanists and Protestants are bolstering up their
respective “ crushings ” of proselytism. It
doesn’t matter an Amen to which sectarian shib-
boleth the dusky victim attaches himself—he
simply exchanges pagan plain for pagan with
trimmings. The more hypocritically adept lie
becomes in mouthing the formulie of a creed which
never gets deeper than his skin, the taller feather
he is in the convertitory cap of Father O’Flynn
or the Reverend Sandy Mae Snuffle. So that
honors are easy, anyhow ! Pagan Plain.

At a wedding celebrated at Ararat (Vic-
toria) lately. Minister : “Wilt thou have this
woman to be thy wedded wife?’’ Bridegroom
(firmly): “I will.” Minister: “ Wilt thou have
this man,” &c. Bride (blushingly): “I will.”
Fisherman (outside): “Fine fresh flatheads!’’
There was not an unmoved countenance in the
church.
• • •
Is there a more aimless, graceless, sense-
less gambol on earth than the Scottish sword-
dance, or reel, or whatever may be the technical
name of that item which a gloomy Caledonian
perpetrates in kilts? The other night, at Melb.
Town Hall, a bare-legged young man placed two
swords crosswise upon the platform, and hopped
monotonously among the four quarterings, whilst
a brither Scot blew beastly noises from the bag-
pipes. The young man was described on the
programme as dancing “Gillie Callum.” Osten-
sibly, he was impersonating a poodle on hot bricks.
Anxiety was written on his face, his body ap-
peared bent with colic, his legs formed two sides
of an equilateral triangle, and worked from the
knees without regard to the squealing of his in-
fernal orchestra. “Gillie Galium” lasted nine
minutes by the clock, and, when it ceased, a voice
from the audience cried, “ Verra guid reel 1 ”
Scandal in England because certain Irish
seats have been bought up for the Liberal party
at .£2OO per annum. It is the old story. If the
State does not pay the members, the members will
pay themselves—or one another.
Mr. Rayner, described as “ a representa-
tive of The Bulletin,” has just figured in a
Maoriland assault-case said to have arisen out of
some remarks about another man’s wife in a
pamphlet issued by him. It is necessary here to
state that Mr. Rayner (who formerly travelled in
Queensland for a brief period as a Bulletin can-
vasser) does not represent this paper in Maori-
land, and has never been authorised by The
Bulletin to say, in that country or elsewhere,
a "vthing about anybody’s wife.
The middle week in July will be sadly re-
membered by Australian miners. On the 18th,
nine men, working in the slopes at the back of the
400 ft. level of the Broken Hill South, were killed
by a concussion of air, caused by a creep in the
workings. On the 20th, six men employed at the
M'Evoy gold and tin mine, Eldorado (Vic.), were
entombed by a similar creep, coming this time
from old workings. Several others, working near
by, would have been killed by the rush of liquid
mullock had not Joseph Ferguson, one of the
heroes so often discovered in mining emergencies,
rushed up the crosscut to them, away from the
shaft and safety, and called them out. They were
just able to get clear, and would have been.over-
taken and smothered had not a huge boulder stuck
in the drive behind them. As it was, they were
wading through mullock up to their necks. And
on the 21st inst., the Broken Hill Block limine
was discovered to be on fire, and remains so at
time of writing.
The Melbourne Government House balls
which have been announced have proved an
incentive to the artistes de toilettes. Much toil
and thought must have been exercised in some
of the productions wherewith to adorn beauty,'
which have lately been seen. One evidence of
rare artistic skill in this way was a faultlessly-
fitting evening gown of lovely rich brocaded silk,
draped in thoroughly Parisian taste, in Messrs.
Hicks, Atkinson and Sons’ establishment. This
was not an imported gown, but the work of their
Court dressmaker, who is recently from Paris,
and who has evidently received a good share of
the gift which made Worth famous in the world
of women.*

And men essay the simple task
Of doing surplus dollars in.
Each horse’s breeding, age, and “weight"
I scanned ; then sought a pal of yore,
Whose tips were tolerably straight—
He’d put me on “ good things ” before.
I found Gamaliel in the bines,
Recovering from rum, alack 1
I named the horses, bade him choose
The likeliest, and best to back.
Bnthe, the Oracle, seemed glum—
Sat sad and silent, in his chair,
Till half a tumblerful of rum
Produced a somewhat brisker air.
Then spake: “ Hard times and biz. forlorn
Have changed these days from days of yor- ,
And Melbourne now, alas ! is shorn
Of old Cup-glories, evermore.
‘We’re stiff as biscuits here in town:
All tiie good chaps are ‘ up a tree ’ —
And more, tiie Cup - with stakes cut down—
Is not the race it used to be !”
He drained ids glass and heaved a sigh,
And o’er tiie list of horses looked ;
Then, slyly; winked his dexter eye
And said, “ I guess ‘ The Cup ’ is cooked!"
Another drink lie drained. Tiie same,
In lesser quantity, I took ;
Whilst inspiration somehow came
From emphasis he laid on “cook.”
“ The horse that’s owned by Sammy Cook ?”
“Guessed well and wisely !” said my pal;
“Get hence, and with some honest ‘book’
Put your ‘ bit ’ on—Tiie Admiral.”
The Admiral’s tiie horse I’m “shook”
Upon ; and for tiie Cup I shall
Place confidence In “Captain Cook”
And dollars on his—Admiral.
THE BREAKER.
It would take more than a fiver to buy Mr.
“Black Bill” Forrester’s foal by Carbine from Rosary
when it arrives.
• * * *
Maliaki, the horse which has won most money
in Maoriland during the season, is owned by a Maori.
* » » »
G. G. Stead, of M.L., intends sending over one
of his Maxim colts, Mannlioher, for the A.J.C. Derby.
Mr. Stead never travels from home unless lie has some-
thing good. When lie brought the sire of Mannlicher
across as a two-year-old, the great Abercorn only beat
him by a bead in the Sires Produce Stakes.
» * » »
Lord Wilton sired three out of five winners at
Moonee Valley (Vic.) races last Week.
• • * »
When, amongst other Royal household appoint-
ments, that of tiie Duke of Portland was cabled recently,
one Australian daily took care that its readers should
know who this person was who had become Master of tiie
Horse. So the elaborated cable read: “The Duke of
Portland (owner of Carbine), Master of the Horse.”
* * ♦ *
A motion in favor of abolishing the “ tote ” has
be -n carried in Maoriland Parliament. The pr ‘Vince has
had lime to forget tiie old pre-tote days when it was
over-run with bookmakers, vvelshers and spielers, and tiie
raw, red-headed Presbyterian element once more asserts
itself, with the Mossy Isaacs brigade shooing it on. How-
ever, Govt, proposes to let the tote alone.
» ♦ * *
“Tom” Manifold, killed in* the hunting-field
last week, was a rich young man and a particular favorite
of Lord and Lady Hopeioun. Though owning many
good horses lie rarely bet, and only had a pound on when
Apsley won the O.N. A good rifle-shot, fine rider, and a
Western district, polo champion, he died in agony at tiie
early age of 30 years. The second amateur killed in the
Vic. hunting-field within a fortnight.
• • • •
An open secret is the expressed and exe-
cuted determination of the owner of a disqualified
Caulfield Cup winner never to allow his prads to finish
behind those of tiie man to whom the Cup was awarded.
The revenge has been carried out. Tiie “ other man ”
has run into second place many times, always to find the
winner supplied by bis enemy.
• • * *
Victorian criticism of the totalisator chiefly
radiates from Bendigo, the city of Alf. Joseph. Aif. and
his appointed legal wire-puller are making themselves
busy everywhere, hut it is from the Bendigo pubs., press,
and pulpit that the voice of Josephus will be oftenest
heard.
There are many points to be noted in connec-
tion with anli-Tote manifestations. Melb Sportsman is
doing its poor little best to uphold the Hebrews, who
support it largely ; and Melb. Herald puls the best
possible face —which is an ugly one at that—on Ikey Mo,
because tiie Sportsman aforesaid represents the ’Erald
stable. The Tote struggle is full of humor for the
initiated.
• • • •
A row looms re the captaincy of next Austra-
lian eleven. The position is by most people assigned to
Geo. Giffen, hut there are many who predict rhat should
he bead the team the results will he dire. Writes one
correspondent; “Everybody knows what an important
factor a good captain is, and everybody who knows any-
thing about Australian cricket knows that Australia
would have won tiie rubber from Stoddart’s team had
Giffen not been our captain. A man so irritable, full of
prejudice, and with suoh’an exalted opinion of his own
prowess, should never be allowed to have charge of an
Australian Eleven. There is one man who is certain to be
in the team who lias all the essentials of a captain— good
temper, excellent judgment of game and men, fairness
and coolness. His claims cannot lie overlooked. Victoria’s
success was due to him last season ; that man is Harry
Troit.” Then (here is a contingent who advocate the
claims of Blackball! as captain, while admitting that as
regards active cricket he has long passed the meridian.
George Giffen says he has not been consulted as
to the personnel of the next Australian team, and know*
nothing about the prospective team under the manage-
ment of Phil. Sheridan. Giffen’s candidate for manager
is “Jack" Creswell, sec. S.A.C.A.
* ♦ * *
English cricket statistics to date explain, in a
measure, the high scores made by Australia against
Stoddart’s team. Richardson, Briggs, Peel, and Look-
wood stood only seventh, tenth, and eighteenth, respec-
tively, on the list of bowling averages, the cracks thus far
being Painter, Rougher, Attewell, and Murch. Peel’s
fahing-off is remarkable.
* • » *
M‘Laren’s innings of 424 against Somersetshire
must surprise everybody who saw that somewhat wan
young man make his two big scores on the Melb. ground.
Although a fine, free, safe bat for awhile after he had
“got set’’—not an everyday occurrence with him —
M’Laren seemed physically incapable of preserving Ins
form through a whole day's play. He made 200 odd
against Victoria, but at the 150 stage he weakened to such
a degree that any good ball wou.d have beaten him, and
for about balf-an-bour bis wicket wasn’t worth five
minutes purchase. In tlie final test-match, M’Laren was
exhausted when he hit bis wicket, so completely done-up
as to suggest the idea that he purposely put himself out
of bis misery.
* * • *
W. G. Grace, says The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, lias reason for pray for ttie soul of the man
who first invented cricket, for tie owes all that lie lias in
the world to it. His two achievements of completing his
liundredtli hundred, and making his 1000 runs in the
first month ot the season, created quite a furore, which has
not quite died out, and a national testimonial is in pro-
gress, led by the Daily Telegraph, the astute managers
of which, seeing a big advt. in it, have started a shilling
subscription. But as people talk of collecting from
£30,000 to £50,000 I lie shillings w ill need to come in faster
and thicker th in they have up to now. A letter from
“ Tummy ” to Grace gave the thing a big fillip, and Col.
North followed this up with a cheque for £5O. The
M.C.C. are to give £lOO, Surrey the same amount, and a
like sum is promised by his own county.
A proposal lias been made to insure Grace’s life
for the benefit of his family to the amount of £20,000,
but I don’t think this will suit VV.G.’s book, as he is fond
of cash, and would much rather have half the amount on
the nail. But whatever is done people must hurry up,
for though the glamour of successive big scores will keep
up enthusiasm, a few small ones will soon damp it, and
already there are signs of W.G. getting out of form. He
fluked up 30, Hie other day, at Lord’s, but ought to have
been caught before he bad scored. In fact, all this season
his luck has been remarkable.
♦ * * ♦
H. H. Massie, having been made an hon,
member of Marylebone C.C., was playing for the club
against Kent at Lord’s when the mail left. He had only made
four when his stumps W'ere scattered ; and he distin-
guished himself in I lie field by missing an easy catch or
two. He received quite an ovation from the crowd and
pavilion, for the recollection of his prowess at the Oval in
’B2 is still fresh. Massie’s manner, too, is liked.
♦ • » »
Trotting now blooms in Maoriland. During the
season Canterbury Trotting Club has given £2OOO and
the Lancaster Park Club ±l6OO in prizes. Starr drove
the Berlin stallion Kentucky balf-a-mile in 1.12 J, and two
miles in 5.9. The times are good considering they were
made on a poor half-mile track.
» * * ♦
The American trotters and pacers are gradually
nearing the coveted 2min. The trotting record now
stands at 2.2 J, and the pacing 2 Tiie latest means
towards tiie end are a couple ot sulky inventions ; one is a
single wheeler; in tiie oilier tiie rider is seated im-
mediately over tiie horse’s back, about where the saddle
would be. The day a horse paces the mile in 2min., the
4th July will cease to be the Yankee National festival.
* * * *
The interest in Sydney football this season
culminated last Saturday in tiie match Randwick v.
Pirates, which 10,000 people gathered to witness. When
the match began the Pirate forwards, though lighter than
the Randwicks, swept down the gr mud W'ith a tierce pace
which threatened the hostile line every second. But it
was behind the forwards that the battle was lost and won.
The feverish excitement spread throughout the Pirat#
team, making their backs eager and anxious, while tba
Rand wick backs preserved a sure and cool defence. Then,
too, there was admirable generalship on the Bandwlck
side. Early in the game the easiest chance possible was
given one of'the Pirate backs, but the demoralising
excitement of his side caused him (a very fine kick) to
miss a goal that many a novice would have scored. There
is one point, however, on which too much praise cannot
be given the Pirates. When the game stood Randwick 6,
Pirate 0, and Duggan, of Randwick, got hurt too badly to
play on, the Pirates allowed a fresh man to take his place.
One man less might have made tiie contest equal ; as it
was Randwick won by 14 points to 0.
* * • •
The football-match on Sydney Cricket Ground
last Saturday, University v. Paddington Electorate,
ended in a hollow win for University by 19 points to'o.
The mile and two-miles cycling handicaps were both won
by Graeme. The scratch men were never in it, and the
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen will have to abandon the
practice of handicapping men out of a race unless they
wish to absolutely kill tire interest. And public interest
in the cash amateur must be kept up to boiling-point
always.
* • • •
Geelong, cocksure of dishing Essendon, was
knocked silly by the latter’s dashing play, and only
recovered in time to find the match lost. At no point of
the game, save high marking in tire third quarter, did
Geelong approach the excellence of EssmdOn, which, In
tire opinion of some good judges, played the best game in
its history.
♦ * * *
Geelong F.O. still heads the premiership list,
having played one more match than Melb.
» * » *
South Adelaide club leads at present for S.A.
football premiership. The other Saturday the “ Sovvths ”
were so far ahead that unless they lost a match, the issue
was beyond doubt for the season. The Souths lost
accordingly.

“Jack’’Harry and A. E. Trott, of E. Melb.
baseball club, are about the two best pitchers in the pro-
vince.
• • » *
Bicycling (writes a Bullktin correspondent
from England) is all the rage just now in swelldom, the
Duchesses of Portland and Manchester (Ihe latter is
really at an age to know better) having set the example.
There is a club in Chelsea where neophytes are put
through their facings before disporting themselves in
Battersea .Park, which is the fashionable rendezvous, and
where the cream of “Society” is to be seen on wheels
every' morning before 12. White’s Club (the real one of
the historical “bow window’’) has a branch establish-
ment there where ladies can breakfast or take refreshers,
and this lias quite given a cachet to the business. It may
interest ladies to know'that “rational” costume is not
worn, all the fair riders being attired in short dresses of
blue serge.
• ♦ ♦ •
The battle between the bat and ball seems now
all in favor of the bat. Grace’s record of 344 rung lias
been beaten by A. C. Maclaren, who, in playing for
Lancashire v. Somersetshire, stayed at the wickets for
7h. 50min., making 424 runs, including 62 fours and 37
twos.
• • • *
Gray’s starting-machine looks like being a big
thing for the inventor. A machine is now on its way to
England, and it lias only to be seen working to make a
certainty of its adoption, even in the conservative “old
country.” It has been patented in France and India.

Immense numbers of wild horses exist in the
Burnett district (Q.). Since last Xmas two men have shot
2400 “ brumbies ” on Knockhreak and Rawbelle stations I
The skins and hair are sent to Southern markets. Most
of the beasts are weeds, but some are splendid animals.

The Melville'lsland shooting-party is going
ahead. They report having only met small herds up to
the present but expect bigger things shortly. They have
alreadv slaughtered some 330 buffaloes—most of the cows
killed being in calf. At this rate the buffalo will soon be
extinct.

The introduction of the Linotype in Melb.
Herald office will cause the dismissal of 44 out of
64 compositors! The Linotype machines in Sydney
Daily Telegraph office have, it is stated, already
almost paid for themselves. The Bulletin
hears, by the way, that it is not true that the
S.M. Herald proprietary thinks of introducing
the Linotype. The story is that the Fairfaxes are
more than satisfied with the Hattersley machines,
and have sent for more.
The last House of Commons had a majority
in favor of Home Rule for Ireland, yet, among 35
old members returned unopposed to the new
House, there were 22 Conservatives and 7
Unionists. The money and family influences
which make seatk “safe” are on the Imperial
Tory side five times out of six.
Joe Thompson named his residence at
Bast Melbourne *‘Don Juan House,” after the
winner of the Melbourne Cup of 1873. Had he
known a few days before the race the condition of
the Don he would have probably never had a
house. A violent attack on the lungs was only
abated and the horse brought round by the unre-
mitting care of a most popular Victorian trainer,
whose good judgment has since been rewarded by
the success of winning the greatest Australian
handicap with a horse of his own breeding.*
Judges want to know if Carbine, who is
selected to cross with St. Simon mare, was a good
investment, why it would not be a good thing to
buy Musket mares to cross with St. Siinon. There
are several at St. Albans, and as La P leche and
Memoir are similarly bred the winners in the
sweep might cable “ Portland.”*

Ihe new extradition treaty with Argentina,

Dear Bulletin, —A suggestion. Why
should there not be a federal Acclimatisation
Board to supervise ami regulate the introduction
ot non-Australasian animals and plants likely to
thrive and be a source of profit to Australasia, to
prevent the introduction of possible pests, and to
em ct the acclimatisation of desirable animals and
plants of one province in another, where possible?
What may happen through unregulated private
enterprise is manifest in the rabbit and fox plagues
throughout the Australias, and in the destructive
effect efforts to reduce these plagues have had
on other useful beasts and birds. What can be
gone by persistent and wise endeavor has already
been shown in several provinces. In Victoria,
we now have fallow' deer on the Upper Yarra, the
Indian axis deer on the Grampians, Sambur deer
in ttie Koo-w'ee-rup swamp district, Rusa and
x ormosan ut-er in the Gembrook ranges, and hares
in (southern Victoria. We have ostriches in
Western Victoria, pheasants in theDandenong dis-
trict, while thrushes, starlings, minahs, and spar-
rows keep down insect-pests all over the country.
On the other hand, the alpaca, which requires
mountainous country IO.OOUft. above sea-level, has
failed for lack thereof, and the cashmere goat from
a similar cause ; also, partridges, Californian
quail, robins, goldfinches, linnets, and other small
birds have practically succumbed to native cats,
tiger cats, iguanas, snakes, laughing jackasses,
hawks, and bush-fires. Trout thrive well, but
English and Californian salmon have failed
utterly. In Maoriland, again, red, axis and fallow
deer, the Ceylon elk, hares, and Tasmanian black
opossums have been successfully acclimatised ; so
have pheasants, partridges, quail, skylarks, black-
birds, thrushes, finches, starlings, minahs, linnets,
a,nd sparrows among birds; and trout, char, white-
fish, perch, tench, and salmon among fish. In
Westralia, also, trout from Tasmania, Murray cod
and Murray perch from South Australia, and
silver eels from Victoria have been successfully
introduced. Lots more can be done in this way
in all the provinces, and a federal acclimatisation
board could do it best of all.— J.S.R.

Buyers and manufacturers interested in
baking and confectionery trades are reminded that
the third annual International Exhibition and
Market, confined exclusively to these important
industries, will be held in London, England, from
September 21 to 28 of this year. All the engineer-
ing and manufacturings firms of repute in England
are making prominent exhibits, and there is]
reason to believe that all the inventions that have
been introduced in recent years will be-.on view.
The Agricultural Hall is the largest building of
its kind in England, and the whole of the Great
Hall is already fully let, and there remains but
little space in the galleries. The attendance last
year was nearly 200,000 people in the week, and
very large commercial transactions were effected
by the exhibitors. The Exhibition has the sup-
port and countenance all the associations con-
nected with the trade in the United Kingdom.*
Mr. W. R. Wilson paid a flying visit to
Sydney, last week, to confer with Mr. George
Adams as to fixing the date of drawing the St.
Alban’s sweep. Tickets are being sold at the
rate of 10,000 a month, and Mr. Adams thinks
this number might be increased threefold if the
date of drawing were announced, but it has been
decided not to do this at present. Mr. Wilson
fears that the requisite number of shares might
not—in spite of Mr. Adams’ opinion to the con-
trary —be taken up on the appointed day.
This would involve his taking a parcel him-
self, and possibly becoming a prize-winner—
which he does n>t want to be. Mr. Adams
says the best periods of the year for selling shares
in these large sweeps are the spring months,
during the sporting season, and at the close of the
shearing.*

“ It la consoling,” writes a Melb, cones-
pendent to Ihb Bulletin, “ for a Melbourne man
who sits at breakfast picking splinters of Ballarat
bacon from between bis teeth, to read in his morn-
| ll g journal that Victoria is likely to establish an
immense bacon-export trade. The local consumer
I Ilever struggles with a bit of badly-cured pig with-
j out wishing it had been exported. Seeing that
i Batls factory ham can be bought from the same
people who grow the tough] ill-flavored rasher of
salt wood, there is surely some giound for the
assertion that first-class Australian bacon is an
impossibility.” Yet they sell in Sydney excellent
V ictonan bacon.

Editor Bulletin, —Re Federation. The
settlers on this river (Dumaresq, Q.) don’t want
Tit. I his year they have sold their tobacco for
7 §d. per lb. all round. In Sydney it is often sold
as low as l^d. —hardly ever higher than sd.
Jfederation would, as far as I can see, destroy this
industry the only one left whereby a few pounds
can be made at farming. But just admire the
madness of the said settlers. Everyone is this
season doing his utmost to place Chinamen on
his land to grow the weed, so that he himself may
sit down and look on. He will take, say, one-
third of the crop; and to be as well off as if he
grew two acres himself, ho must get the Chinese
to grow six acres. One hundred farmers growing
600 a. tobacco by Chinese will flood the local
market and reduce prices to N.S.W. level, whilst
100 J farmers growing only 200 a. would be safe
and have all the profits, and not overstock the
market. There is no outlet for leaf, as every
province and country levies heavy import duties.
Already we may call this New Mongolia.—H.

When the M.L. Tariff Commission visited
Brunnerton (West Coast) lately, the local Mayor,
who chaperoned it, pointing to the cliff across
the bridge, called attention to a mass of fallen
rocks and debris and the remains of a house which
have been turned upside-down, and delivered him-
self thus: “ There were 40 people in that house one
night last week holding a wake, when the slip
occurred and knocked them all topsy-turvy, yet
the only one who was not injured was the corpse.
The room he was in was not smashed like the
other rooms, but the mercy is, with all them
boulders coming through, some of them were not
killed, though they were nearly all smashed a bit
and bruised.”
There has been much mysterious shaking
of heads lately amongst fat persons—who know
all about Boaz and Tubal-Cain, and who each
month walk around the grave of Hiram Abiff with
their fingers to their noses—over a recent N.S.W.
country-town incident. A newly-arrived civil
servant, who doesn’t belong to the craft, had put
up at the leading hotel, the headquarters of the
local Masonic lodge. The barnacle’s bedroom
was next to the lodge-room, and for many years
had been used on lodge-nights by the mystic
brotherhood as a sort of ante-room to the lodge—
a department where preliminary mysteries were
conducted, where quaking novices were put
through their paces, where, maybe, the Gulielmus
Capricornicus of the lodge found temporary stable
accommodation, and where—this at any rate is a
certainty—the members were in the habit of
depositing their hats and overcoats. On lodge-
night the civil servant, after a nightcap at the
bar, proceeded to his bedroom, near the door
of which, to his great mystification, he found a
functionary standing, sword in hand. The C.S.
pushed past this potentate—the tyler of the lodge
—and proceeded to open his bedroom door. The
tyler, in terrible accents, said : “ You cannot enter
there !” and made a menacing movement. Where-
upon the C.S., an athletic personage, closed with
the guard, and, having thrown him downstairs,
entered his room, to find it filled with 50 or 60
different varieties of hats, &o. These he promptly
pitched out of the window, together with some of
the lodge-paraphernalia, and so completely vindi-
cated his position as sole tenant of the room that
if the lodge billy-goat wasn’t found in the public
pound next day it wasn’t his fault. The Masons
are horrified at the outrage, and a complaint
having been made to the head of the civil servant’s
department, he has been called upon for a report.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
A first-class smoking-carriage on the Hob-
son’s Bay line (Vic.) was occupied the other even-
ing by one gaudy dude, in a fur-lined coat and a
top-hat. Just as the train was starting, a work-
man scrambled in. The top-hatted one glared at
him with the disgusted expression of a man who
wasn’t accustomed to such beastly low company,
and ostentatiously lighted a sixpenny cigar to
purify the atmosphere. The workman stared
back, and retaliated with a short pipe. Then the
superior person moved to the further end of the
carriage, and crowded himself into the corner till
they got to Windsor, when he waved his cane out
of the window and called a “ portah.” “This—
haw—person haft intruded himself here—l believe,
without any authority whatevah. Would you—
haw—inspect his ticket?” The workman pro-
duced a first-class ticket, and said, curtly ;
“Pr’aps you’ll be so kind as to inspect his
ticket now ? ” Then there was a long delay while
the lofty person felt among his high-priced clothes,
and at last reluctantly handed out a second-class
season. “You’ll have to get out of here, sir,”
said the official, in an unsympathetic voice.
“Shall I help you to chuck him out?” said the
workman. Then a door banged loudly, and a cold
gust of wind blew the top-hat along the platform.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.

Sant by some satirical dog:— Bulletin
man (noticing highly-decomposed human head on
St. Kilda beach); “Ah ! Here’s a story for the
Christmas number 1”
The first Sydney Sunday newspaper was
the Sydney Gazrttb of 2nd April, 1803. The
change from Saturday to Sunday was made so
that a whole week’s news and shipping should
appear. It remained a Sunday paper for seven
years.
In Australasia hanging is the commonest
method of suicide among men, shooting comes
second, cutting, stabbing and drowning tie
for third place, while poisoning stands fourth.
Women go mostly for drowning and poisoning,
often for stabbing or hanging, hardly ever for
shooting.

of every child born and the date of its birth.”—
