The Obstinacy of Mrs. Tatton.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were lying off the town of Niafu, in
Vavau, one of the Friendlies, when Tatton came
on board. He was a short, dark, thick-set
man, and master of a small trading vessel, the
Lunahlo. We had made his acquaintance at
Wallis Island about a year previously, and on the
sain© day that we ran into Yavau the Lunalilo
followed us, towed in by her boat. As she passed
our vessel, Tatton, whom we saw standing aft,
waved his hand and called out to me to come on
board after supper, and the skipper and myself
were about getting ready when the steward came
to say
“ Here’s Captain Tatton, sir.”
We jumped up and shook hands with him. Our
skipper, a rough, hard-shell, old Connecticut
Yankee, with a heavy hand and a soft heart, at
once blurted out —
“ What the blazes is the matter with you,
Tatton ? Got yaller fever or the cholery morbus
aboard ? Anyone been and run away with your
little missus, or what ?”
Tatton attempted to smile at old Barron’s joke,
but failed. .
“No one has run away with Luisa, Barron,
but she’s leaving me, all the same. The poor girl’s
dying. I came in here thinking I would find the
Narrangansett. She passed me last night, heading
this way, and steaming very fast. I burnt every
blue-light I had on the schooner, and fired a gun ;
but she was too far off, I suppose,” and the
sturdy, hard - faced seaman passed his hand
Wearily over his face.
In an instant Barron, the grizzled old veteran
of 30 years’ hardship and adventure in the two
Pacifies, reached out his hand to Tatton.
“ That’s bad news. Is there anything we can
do for you, Tatton? I guess you reckoned on the
Narrangansett’s doctor. Is she very bad ?”
Tatton nodded. “ Dying. I can see that; and
I think she knows it, too. Only a doctor can be
of any use. What can I do? I wonder if the
Yankee has gone on to Tongatabu ? If I thought
so I would heave up and try and get there in
time.” ~ , . ,< ,
“ Wait till to-morrow,” said our skipper, she
thay turn up by then. And they told us in Apia
that they were coming here first.”
This cheered Tatton up a bit; and, after a glass
of grog, he asked me to go back with him. Before
leaving the cabin our skipper said to me
1 “ Got any champagne left in the trade-room ? ’
“ About half-a-case.’’
“ All right, put it down against me”—and when
the steward handed down a heavy native basket
into Tatton’s boat I knew what it was, and whom
it was for. And I remembered that when Tatton,
Barron, and myself were involved in an angry
argument over a.yam-trading incident at Futuna,
our visitor had told the grizzled old skipper “ he
was too blasted mean to live, like all Yanks.
# * *
.Tatton’s schooner, like our own vessel,
lay close in to the shore, and the distance between
them was short. He was very silent, and I could
she he was deeply touched at the prospect of losing
his wife. About a year or so before we had met
him at Wallis Island, he had married the daughter
o£_an old trader living on one of the Navigators
Group—a delicate-looking, child-like creature,
who, were she in civilisation, would hardly have
left the nursery. And since then Tatton, the
hard-drinking, quarrelsome skipper whose chief
argument in any dispute was his fist —and he had
many arguments, in which native women were
generally involved—had undergone a wonderful
change and acquired an extraordinary renown ;
in brief, he became that rare fiah in any sea, a
moral trading captain.
• # •
Luisa, born of a Manhikian mother by a
white father, was lying on a bed of soft mats
spread on the cabin floor. By her side was a
native sailor fanning her, for the cabin was close.
Seated on the transoms a few feet further off was
another seaman, a big, sallow-faced native of
Mauhiki. He was nursing a baby—a baby with
a face like a toad. He looked stolidly at us for a
moment, then bent his face over that of the sleep-
ing child.
“ She is asleep,” said the man beside her in a
whisper. Tatton silently motioned me to a seat,
and we spoke in whispers.
“ Born just as we passed Beveridge Reef a week
ago,” and he pointed over to where the big Man-
hiki man sat solemnly swaying to and fro.
“ She wanted to be away from Aitutaki
pined co get back to Samoa to her mother and
her people. So I closed up the station and
brought her aboard. Such rotten luck you never
saw. Head winds and calms, calms and head
winds, for nearly a month ; and then, just off
Beveridge . . .”
Here the girl moved and awoke.
“ Luisa,” said Tatton, bending over her, “here
is'an old friend of your father’s.”
The girl looked at me; then put out her slender
hand and said in her mother’s tongue, in a voice
scarce above a whisper, “ Ah, yes, I remember
you. Have you forgotten the day when you and
my father and brothers went to Apia to the
fa’atau fui (auction) and Alafoti, the big American
who rapped on the table with a hammer, gave me
a dressed-up doll? ’’—and she smiled faintly.
m * *
That was over six years ago. How it all
came back to me ! Fat, jolly old Alvora, sell-
ing a miscellaneous lot of goods in an Apian resi-
dent’s house, and the strange, motley crowd that
surrounded him ; among them this girl s father,
his sons and myself. And I remembered, too, a
little girl of about ten coming in at the door,
dressed in European style, and smiling at us ; and
old Ned bringing her over to me and telling me it
was his little girl “ runned down from the Sisters
where she was a-sohoolin’ to see her brothers;
then when the faatau tai was over how the old
dried-up trader, his stalwart sons, the little girl
and myself all walked up to the French Mission
and gave the runaway back to the good Sisters.
And here she w is now, not yet much past sixteen,
a mother, and dying.
The big sailor cirae over beside her and squatted
cross-legged on the mats, latton placed his arm
around her and raised her up to look at the
hideous little bundle of mortality in his arms.
“ What an ugly little aitu (devil) it is,” she said
to Tatton in Samoan, as the Manhiki man placed
it on the mat within touch.
Tatton turned to me with something like a
smile. “By God, she’s pulling herself together
again. If that cruiser would only show up Id
give the ship and cargo.” Then he went and
opened the wine and gave her a glass.
I stayed another hour or so, and then left them;
bearing in my mind the picture of the slight
figure of the girl, who had again fallen asleep
under the effects of the wine, lying motionless on
the couch of mats; the big man-nurse, and
Puniola, the Savage Island sailor, softly waving
his fan over the wan features; and Tatton, with
his sun-browned face, resting on his hand, gazing
intently down upon the sleeper.
• • •
The grey mists of dawn had scarcely
begun to shift from the hills behind Niafu
when we saw the long black hull °f the
Narrangansett steaming up the harbor, bne
was one of the famed “ ninety - day ships,
and made a brave show as she cut aside the calm
waters and brought up a couple of hundred yards
astern of us. Her anchor had barely touched the
ground when we saw Tatton s boat pull alongside,
and in another five minutes leave again with
another man seated beside Tatton, and pull hard
for the Lunalilo. ~ , „ . , ,
“ Waal, now, look at that,” said the Grizzled
One to me, as we sat sipping our coffee on the
skylight; “there’s a man that, for the past ten
years, has been up to all kind of red-hot oussed-
ness, plain and decorated, nigger-catchin , women-
buyin’, and sioh like Island fixin’s, and, as sure as
I ain’t one of the saved, but he’s jest a turned
man, all over that slip of a yaller girl. Laud
alive ! but he must think a lot of her, to have the
front to pull the doctor outer his bunk before
coffee in the morning ! ”
» • •
We had finished our coffee and were
watching the movements of those on board the
steamer when we saw the Lunalilo s boat again
leave and pull over to us, with only two men
in her. They bumped up alongside, and one
jumped on deck and gave me a note from iatton,
“ Come on board as quick as you can. Bring
Allan with you.” .
Allan was our boatswain, a M inhiki half-caste.
Wondering what was wrong, I called him, and we
got in the boat and went aboard. The moment
we gained the deck Tatton met us, looking pale
and excited. The doctor of the man-of-war was
sitting on deck smoking a cigar.
“ How is she ?” I asked Tatton.
“ Bad, rny lad ; and the doctor says that unless
he can attend to her at once she cannot possibly
live more than a few days.”
“Well,” I asked wonderingly, “why doesn t
he?” „ ......
“ Because she won’t let him. Says she d rather
die ten times over first. You know what a curious
sort of modesty native women have about some
things. Well, as soon as ever the doctor came on
board—of course I’d let him know as far as my
knowledge went what was wrong—l told her he
would soon put her right. She sat up and com-
menced to cry, and said she wouldn’t have him; and
the moment I went on deck to call the doctor down
that big Manhiki buck lifted her up and carried
her into my cabin, put the youngster in with her,
and then locked the door. Now, he’s standing
guard outside. The fool says he’ll kill anyone
that tries to open it. You see he’s a kind of a
far-away cousin of the family on the mother s side.
That’s why I asked you to bring Allan. Perhaps
he can talk Rimi over into ...”
Allan shook his head. “ It’s no use, Captain
Tatton,” he said in excellent English. “If you
like I’ll go down and scruff Rimi and sling him on
deck ; but, by God, I’ll take his place if your wife
wants me to keep out the doctor.”
“Curse you for a wooden-headed Kanaka,
said Tatton ; “ don’t I tell you she’s got to die if
she won’t see the doctor ?”
“Look here, Captain Tatton,’ said the half-oaste
boatswain again, “ you ought to know enough of
native ways by this time to know that no man can
aid your wife. Take her ashore here to some of
the old Tongan women. She’d be disgraced for
life if you force a doctor on her, and she knows
ifc ’’ .
Poor Tatton was half mad. We went to
the man-of-war doctor and explained. He was a
good-natured man, and listened quietly.
“ I will wait here another hour—two hours, he
said, “if you think she will change her mind. If
she won’t I think you can’t do better than take
this man’s advice,” pointing to Allan, “ and let
her be attended by some native women. They
may save her life, but I doubt it. It’s a surgical
case.”
Then he got up and walked to the rail.
* • •
Allan went down, and his huge country-
man and he talked. Then Allan called out to us to
come down, except the doctor, and the big man
opened the door and let us in. Luisa was lying
in Tatton’s bunk, clasping his hideous little effigy
to her bosom. ...
“Luisa,” said Tatton, placing his hand on her
arm, and speaking in English, “do you under-
stand that if you will not let the American fo mat
attend to you that you will die ? Is it not so ? ’
turning to Allan and me.
The girl’s big, frightened eyes sought ours to
read the answer, and then slowly closed. She lay
quite still a moment or so while the tears welled
out and coursed down her cheeks.
“ E pule le Atua ,” she said at last. (“ It is God s
will if I die.”)
“Luisa,” I said, “don’t you want to see your
brothers and sisters again? Why are you ashamed?
In papalagi (the white man’s land), when a child
is born or a woman is sick to death, it is the cus-
tom of all women to have with them a fo'mai to
save them from death.”
She shook her head. “ I know. Tatton hath
told me that many times. But what woman but a
shameless white woman would suffer such a thing?”
• * *
The doctor’s step sounded overhead. Ritni,
with a dangerous look in his eye, shoved past us
and stood at the head of the bunk. Allan, speak-
ing in Manhikian to him, said, “Have no fear,’
and he went out again. Poor devil, a perfect
slave to Tatton at any other time, he was ready to
lay his life down in defence of this ever-so-distanl
cousin before she should be “ made ashamed.”
Then we went on deck again, defeated. The
doctor said he would send some medicine—all he
could do. As he stood in the gangway lighting
another cigar he said, in answer to Tatton : “ Oh,
yes ; give her a glass of champagne now and then ;
it’ll keep her alive a little longer and do no harm.”
I went away with the doctor, leaving Allan to
help Tatton take his wife ashore to the native she-
doctors of Niafu village.
* • • 1
Four days afterwards Luisa died. After
the burial Tatton went off to the Narrangansett,
and the doctor improvised a cunningly-contrived
feeding-bottle with a thick rubber tube for the
little Tatton, and gave a couple of dozen tins of
milk “to tucker the kid,” as Tatton expressed it,
“ till he could leave it with its mother’s folks.”

“,I will endeavor,” was the only answer
that a Castlemaiue (Vic.) parson could get from
an intending husband in answer to the usual
“love, cherish, and protect” marriage-service
question. The lady was more certain of herself.
Condensed from Norman Chronicle ; An
aboriginal describes how he secures a feed of
chicken occasionally. “Me chop im wood longa
house. Buneby, me ait down alonga ground and
eatem bread. Fowl come ’long—pick up crumb.
Me take-em lille stone —hit im longa cobra.,
Bimeby, missis say, ‘ What make-em that fellow
powl sick ? ’ Me say me not know. Then missis
say, ‘You take-em that fellow alonga camp and
eatim. ’” Ah-h-h !
Dear Bulletin, —From my brother, out
back: “ The Bulletins reach me in sad repair.
First, the postmaster opens them just to see they
are all right. Then the policeman takes them
home for security, like. The chap with the station-
bag looks upon all papers as his property for the
time being ; the boss and overseer take a squint on
the sly. Paddy cadges them from someone and
reads them to the whole hut, every word, from
articles to book-dept. The boundary-rider brings
them out here, and has them off by heart. When
the last lot came Dan and I patched them up
and struggled through them, and two swaggies
got them and took them down the track to Heaven
knows where. Those Bulletins are well read.”
At the recent opening of the new Rich-
mond-South Yarra Bridge, a spread was given by
the local mayors, and at the conclusion, a caterer’s
assistant, finding one of the guests with a bottle
of wine in his possession, had him locked up on a
charge of theft—which was dismissed at Court,
there being no proof of felonious intent. But
things turned out otherwise some months back,
at a big Northern wedding between the son of a
local magnate and a district heiress. The eere :
mony was a marvellous and magnificent affair,
and the local boomsters and bung-bank directors
tumbled over each other thereat. Suddenly a
would-be funny youth, seeing a very heavy swell
leavipg a side-gate with a small parcel under his
arm, called out: “I see yer! He’s sneaking
some of the presents.” It turned out the
true word spoken in jest, and the bold
peculator, becoming confused, admitted his
guilt in the drawing-room before the assembled
guests. The young couple were “ off ” a prosecu-
tion, but the biggest boomster present stepped
forward and said that the rights of property must
be respected, and a duty was owed to society,
thereupon sending for a policemen. At the trial
of the larcenist the fact was carefully omitted
that he was a guest at the wedding, all the evi-
dence of the prosecution making it appear as if he
had sneaked in by the back-gate ; but the prisoner
knocked all this in a heap by getting into the box
to testify on his own behalf, and swearing that he
had smiled upon the bride’s family when they
wre but at the outer portals of Sassiety. Then
toe bride took hysterica on the floor of the court,
and her mother burst into a series of heart-rending
sobs, and all was chaos for a few seconds until the
presiding beak summed up sufficient presence of
mind to yell out “six months.”
Self-Sacrifice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The thing,” said Philos, “is too one-sided to
bear discussion. It is always viewed telescopi-
cally—the saorificer looking through the orthodox
end, the ‘ sacrificiary ’ through the other. We
cannot even discuss the relative values of the
different kinds of sacrifice, for the value is created
by the circumstances. Still I think its depths
are sounded in the text running, ‘ Greater love
hath no man than this, that he lay down his life
for his friend.’ I call my story—
Two Telegrams.
“ Wish I was home, Tom ! First Christmas
Day I’ve been away since the old man died.
Reckon the old mother’ll grieve a bit to-day.
Damned if I’d a-taken the mob if I’d thought of
it. Let’s send her a wire, Tom—it’ll show I
thought of her anyhow, and cheer her up a bit.
There’s an office just ahead. Hey, Mister ! Shop
shut? I want to send the old woman a merry
Xmas. What ? closed ! Oh, well, send it along
in the morning, will you? Thanks. Have a
nip?”
“Steady, Tom! Ease the bandage, will you?
No go, old boy, it’s all up. That steer was too
many for us. I said when we first yarded him
he’d give us trouble. Just as well I cut in when
I did or you’d a-gone to the Never without time
to pass your swag. Ugh ! makes you feel creepy
to see your own blood coming out like that.
Wonder if mother’s got the message yet ? Sorry
I sent it. Feel a bit dozy—think—l’ll have—a—
a —a .”
“Did you send my mate’s telegram ? J ust sent
it? Well, that’s hard luck. Great Scott! what a
shock for the old woman. Here, I want you to
send another to tell his mother that he saved my
life at the cost of his own. He was killed this
morning.”
* * •
“So you think the greatest sacrifice is that of
life,” said the Pessimist.
“I don’t. Life just now is considerably dis-
counted by the trouble of living, and a man who
has a chance of putting an end to himself with
the honor of heroism instead of the stigma of
suicide is to be envied. We value most that
we have not. To me the highest sacrifice is that
of honor.”
“Pooh,” said Philos, “what is honor but an
idealistic bond of mutual self-protection?”
“Yes,” added the Simpleton, “and the loss of
an ideal is less costly than trying to live up to it.”
“Still,” said the Pessimist, “most men would
face death rather than universal execration—the
coward that wouldn’t doesn’t affect my theory—
his life has a negative value. I call my story
Just Retribution?
“ Was it without reason he black-legged ? With
a sick wife and six children—the eldest nine—to
keep on 10s. a week strike-pay ! You would have
gone to the pub. and forgotten them while you
drank to Unionism ! Ah, well! he didn’t. What
were your taunts to the hunger-glint in their
eyes? Their skin-clad bones cut deeper than
your stones ! And because he dared moral suicide
rather than actual murder you called down the
curses of the Almighty ! You stoned him to the
pit’s mouth ! You prayed the good God he might
never come out alive! Would you have thus
blasphemed had you known ’twould be even so?
And the coincidence you call retribution ! Bah !
The annals of Black-leg-land show brighter heroes
than your selfish souls dream of ! ”
• # »
“ How many of your heroes would have dared
their deeds had they known the probability of
futility—that their sacrifice was vain?” asked
the Simpleton. “ Here’s my own story : I was
in love once—in fact, I still am! Not with the
girl that is, but with the girl I thought she was !
You fellows, of course, can’t understand it, but
the sacrifice of life and honor for her sake would
have been easy. Why, my dead self was sacri-
ficed ! Yet she loved me, too—at the time ! But
there was an obstacle—chimerical, I thought it.
She held it unsurpassable, and so great was my
infatuation that I loved her the more for her high
principles. (Smile, Philos ! ’Tis a common varia-
tion of the world-old story. Only this—l do not
as usual claim pre-eminence in grief). Circum-
stances were such that my presence even occasion-
ally made her lot the harder. I left. Only the
felon who voluntarily closes his eye to the
single ray that for an instant illumines
his darkness can feel as I did. Still it
was for her sake—l was helping her. But
at last my pent-up passion leaped its bounds—l
knew I must see her or go mad. I saw her. The
weary months that had seemed an eternity in the
passing were retrospectively so many hours. She
held out her hand smilingly. ‘I don’t think I’ve
seen you since my wedding,’ she said.
“ Ah f my friends ! worship self-sacrifice if you
will, but, believe me, self-abnegation is the highest
form of altruism.”
PONTE.
THE DUKE OF CAMBRIDGE
O, noble sham ! O, hero grey
Of many battles bloody,
From which you bravely stayed away
Your health and ease to study.
They’ve cut you off—the army’s head !
It might—O, royal bummer !
Have been much worse—they might, instead,
Have sacked a little drummer !
A N.S.W. country pressman : The plain-
tiff in a threatened libel-action seldom has any
cash, and merely goes to law for what he can get.
If the scribe fails to file a defence, plaintiff will
get a verdict by default; if he does file, the action
is dropped and the defendant is a lot out of
pocket. Whether the action comes to a head or
not, the newspaper-proprietor stands to lose.
During the past six months I’ve had four writs
served on me, and in no one case could plaintiff
have won his case. Yet the luxury cost me nearly
.£5O. —X
Dear Bulletin, —Since the ss. per night
travelling allowance for N.S.W. constables came
into vogue (simultaneously with reduction of the
officers’ allowance), the officers leave no stone un-
turned to disprove the bona fides of the claims
made by their subordinates. ' For instance, if a
constable remains the night at a private house
where no actual pecuniary charge is made, but
where the constable has to recompense his enter-
tainers in some other way, no allowance is paid.
M.’sP., who considered they were doing the poor
bobby a good turn by getting him a shilling extra
per night, might well give this matter a little
consideration.— Trooper.
It is now practically impossible for anyone
to distinguish between English Stilton cheese and
the Maoriland imitation thereof; and a N.S. W.
Frenchman who has embarked in the manufacture
of imitation Camembert cheese has get so very
close to the original article that he is making a
little fortune.
How a Pekin editor declines MS. :
Illustrious brother of the sun and moon.—Behold thj
servant prostrate before thy feet. I kowtow to thee, and
beg that of thy graciousness thou mayst grant that 1 may
speak and live. Thy honored manuscript has deigned to
cast the light of its august countenance upon us. With
raptures we have perused it. By the bones of my
ancestors, never have I encountered such wit, such pal bos,
such lofty thought. With fear and trembling I return the
writing. Were Ito publish the treasure you sent me, the
Emperor would order that it should be made the standard,
and that none be published except such as equalled it.
Knowing literature as I do, and that it would be impos-
sible in ten thousand years to equal what you have done,
1 send your writing back. Ten thousand times I crave
your pardon. Behold, my head is at your feet. Do what
you will. Your servant’s servant.— Tub Editor.
The Chinese equivalent of “ returned with
thanks.”
A London correspondent :—Reading in
a recent Bulletin about old Tom Stubbs,
the auctioneer (grandfather of Miss Maud Fitz-
Stubbs), reminds me that the old gentleman
carried on business in King-street, Sydney, for
many years before he went to Melbourne. Even
then he was celebrated for his florid ads.,
which put George Robins in the shade. Mr.
Stubbs was a great admirer of Napoleon Bona*
parte, and called his property on the Parramatta
River (at Tarban Creek) Longwood. It was sold
to the Marists and is now known as Villa Maria.
All over the house could be seen statues, busts,
and statuettes of the Little Corporal, and I have
in my possession a small bust of him which Stubbs
gave to my father. “Old Tom,” as he was called,
was a great musician, a gift inherited by all his
family without exception. Two of his sons—Bob
(father of the clever Maud) and Napoleon, the
second son—started from some China port for
California in the time of the gold mania—l
think in ’49. They themselves were on board
a cutter called the Rose of Australia; and
they convoyed a real Chinese junk, from which
they became separated in a typhoon. The
junk was never heard of, but the cutter was
picked up at sea dismasted with only the two
Stubbs boys on board, both lashed to the vessel.
They were in the last stage of exhaustion, but
they recovered and were brought on to Sydney.
I recollect well their arrival and the excitement
their adventures caused. One of the boys married a
lady who had had the awful experience of being
actually in bed with her adopted mother, Mrs.
Hoadley, in a house in King-st., between George
and York streets, when a man entered the bed-
room and murdered the old lady, the little girl
being all the time hidden under the bed-clothes.
She heard the blows of the hammer with which
the murderer broke Mrs. Hoadley’s skull. All
the Stubbs boys were handsome, fine-looking
fellows, and the daughter, Mrs. Walter Church,
was quite a beauty as a girl.
